Funds Outpay Banks To Lure Data Experts

By Rachael Levy 
451 words
30 December 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Hedge funds are paying top dollar to bring in new employees with quantitative skills, underscoring the desperation some funds face to bulk up their abilities around big data and algorithms.

A new survey from Baruch College's financial engineering program found that recent graduates working at hedge funds made significantly more than their peers working at banks. The master's program at Baruch teaches students skills like data science and financial modeling.

At hedge funds, Baruch graduates' annual pay ranged from around $200,000 to more than $1 million, the survey found. But for those alumni working at banks, pay ranged from about $100,000 to $400,000 a year.

Hedge-fund managers once reigned over the investment industry. Now, many of them are floundering. In the quest to gain a new foothold, many have shifted investment decisions from humans to computers. In turn, Wall Street recruiters say people with quantitative skills are in high demand.

In a sign of the shift, some prominent funds have tried to remake themselves. Funds have been recruiting the kind of people who can make investment decisions using code, big data and machine learning.

Two of the Baruch graduates working at hedge funds reported a compensation of $1 million or more.

"People who make that much money make a lot more money for the firm," said Dan Stefanica, a Baruch math professor who analyzed the alumni salary data. "And if you don't compensate them enough, they just go somewhere else."

Hedge funds' hunger for talent is apparent during Baruch's recruiting sessions. During the fall semester, Wall Street firms make their pitch to students. Three years ago, the split of companies presenting was 75% banks, 25% hedge funds, said Mr. Stefanica.

The survey found female graduates made less than the men, said Mr. Stefanica. Median pay was $190,000 for women and $210,000 for men, he said. Some male graduates surveyed took home more than double the pay than any of the women. For women, pay ranged from $110,000 to $450,000; for men, $110,000 to more than $1 million, he said.

The women graduates surveyed tended to work at banks. More than half of the women -- 64% -- worked in banks whereas 21% worked at hedge funds or in asset management, Mr. Stefanica said.

Across the entire survey, 48% of all respondents worked at banks and 38% worked in asset management or hedge funds. Just under a third of the Baruch survey respondents were women, he said.

Baruch surveyed 50 alumni of the program who have been working for four to six years.

WSJ Pro
Builders VC Expands Leadership, Support for Portfolio Companies; Venture firm adds in-house mental-health professional and seasoned tech executives to aid founders in early stages of company building

By Heather Mack 
630 words
26 December 2019
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

As venture-capital firm Builders VC was preparing to raise its second fund earlier this year, the partners realized they also wanted to secure another type of resource: people.

The San Francisco-based firm, which invests in early-stage companies modernizing industries such as agriculture, construction and health care, has brought on a team of operating partners to provide expertise and leadership to portfolio companies.

Builders, which is investing out of its first $175 million fund and is on track to raise its second fund in 2020, decided to pool money from its partners to build out a team to better support its startups. At a time when capital for startups is plentiful, the move is designed to help differentiate Builders from the slew of emerging early-stage venture funds.

The team, called B2, consists of an in-house network of experts such as a mental-health coach, data and marketing scientists, and public-relations specialists.

B2 also offers a suite of technological resources that most early-stage companies can't afford or can't build in house, such as sophisticated analytics and machine-learning tools.

Operating partners of the B2 team include quantitative marketing expert Isaac Dinner, who currently works as a data scientist at Airbnb Inc.; Praveen Mandal, a data scientist who is currently the chief technology officer at electric-car charging company Volta Charging; and veteran PR specialist Mojgan Khalili, a former Khosla Ventures partner who led communications at Yahoo and Cisco Systems Inc. and now runs Cherry Communications.

Builders General Partner Jim Kim, who founded the firm in 2016, said the goal is to provide the kind of support that founders need but that isn't readily available even in today's capital-flush funding ecosystem.

"The idea is to help entrepreneurs by really addressing the pain points that aren't necessarily solved with more money," said Mr. Kim, a former Khosla Ventures partner who also founded venture firm Formation 8 and remains a general partner there. "There are a lot of fires to put out, and any one of them can be a reason a company fails."

Mr. Kim said the quotidian challenges of running a startup are especially pronounced among Builders VC's portfolio companies, which are working in industries slow to adopt new technology in the first place. The firm has backed startups such as agriculture software provider Performance Livestock Analytics Inc. and long-haul freight technology startup KeepTruckin Inc.

At the same time, he said, this extra set of resources helps to differentiate Builders VC from the hundreds of other early-stage venture firms it competes with.

Mr. Kim referred to B2 as a studio, although it differs from startup studios such as Atomic VC and Science. Those firms are more involved in creating companies and incubating startups in house, while Builders focuses on investing in outside companies.

Other firms, like newly launched Greatscale Ventures, employ a hands-on approach to company-building by taking top executive leadership roles and handling strategic decision-making until the startup is mature enough to do so on its own.

In addition to tactical advice on things like international business development and customer acquisition, founders need empathy about the everyday struggles of running a company, Mr. Kim said.

"These are people who are balancing major life events like having a kid with things like firing a co-founder and recruiting an engineering team," said Mr. Kim. "At the same time, they are hearing that every other startup around them is  killing it.'"

Write to Heather Mack at "mailto:Heather.Mack@wsj.com"

Startups Aim to Bring Gene Therapy to Millions; DraftKings to Go Public in Reverse Merger; Aleph Closes $200 Million Fund

By Tomio Geron 
1,178 words

WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. Gene therapy was once a treatment reserved only for rare diseases. But now, much larger populations that are affected by cancer or neurological diseases could benefit from it. These new approaches from several venture-backed startups could pose a significant challenge to existing large incumbents in the market, experts say.

Meanwhile, sports betting outfit DraftKings plans to go public next year in a reverse merger. The company has gone through many changes over the years, and at one time almost merged with rival FanDuel. Now DraftKings says it will have a market value of $3.3 billion upon closing of the deal.

Note: In observance of the holidays, WSJ Pro Venture Capital will publish its next edition on Monday, Dec. 30. We will still be publishing alerts on significant stories through Dec. 30.

And now on to the news...

Top News

Gene therapy for the masses. After developing the first gene therapies for rare diseases, biotechnology companies are seeking to extend gene treatments to common diseases affecting millions of people, WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley reports. Several gene-therapy startups are using new technologies that could enable them to compete with large drugmakers in giant markets such as neurological disease and cancer.

* This could mean a much larger impact than has been seen so far from gene therapy and has the potential to transform how major diseases are treated. It also could mean major wins for venture investors in these startups.

* For decades, scientists struggled to make good on the tremendous promise of gene therapy, which involves using genes to cause cells in the body to make disease-fighting proteins. Recently, those efforts have paid off.

* U.S. regulators approved the first gene therapy, Luxturna, a treatment for a rare, blinding condition, in December 2017, and in May 2019, they gave the green light to Zolgensma, which treats the genetic disorder spinal muscular atrophy.

"If gene therapies end up working in large indications, they will disrupt the large players."

  Gbola Amusa, partner and head of health-care research at investment bank Chardan Capital Markets LLC

Going public. DraftKings reached an agreement that would take the sports-betting company public next year, a deal that comes as states move to legalize that form of gambling, The Wall Street Journal reports. Best known for its fantasy sports games, DraftKings said Monday it will merge with Diamond Eagle Acquisition Corp., a so-called blank-check company that trades on the Nasdaq and is backed by former MGM movie studio Chief Executive Harry Sloan. DraftKings also struck a deal to merge with gambling-technology provider SBTech. Several investors, including funds managed by Capital Research & Management Co., Wellington Management Co. and Franklin Templeton, have agreed to invest $304 million in a class of the combined company's stock. DraftKings expects the combined company to have a market value of $3.3 billion at closing and said it would have more than $500 million in unrestricted cash on hand. DraftKings' past venture investors included Accomplice VC, GGV, Redpoint and Revolution.

Targets. Payments company PayPal will continue to pursue potential takeover targets in 2020 amid robust deal activity in the payments sector. "There's a lot of opportunities to acquire companies inorganically," CFO John Rainey said in an interview.

$40 Billion

DraftKings' estimate of the potential size of the U.S. market for online sports betting and other games, like those typically found in casinos, according to a recent investor presentation.

Industry News

Israeli investor Aleph closed its third fund with $200 million. The firm recently invested in employee engagement and performance management platform Centrical, log analytics startup Coralogix and third-party security lifecycle management provider Panorays.

Canapi Ventures raised $54.3 million toward a $100 million fund, according to a regulatory filing. With offices in Washington, D.C., and Wilmington, N.C., the firm invests in early- to growth-stage financial technology companies.

Exits

NCR Corp. purchased Zynstra, a provider of back and front office virtualization technology, for about 100 million ($129 million). The U.K.-based company was backed by investors including Octopus Ventures and Kreos Capital.

Mastercard agreed to acquire RiskRecon for an undisclosed amount. The Salt Lake City- and Boston-based cyber risk manager has raised more than $40 million in funding from investors including Accel, Dell Technologies Capital, General Catalyst and F-Prime Capital.

Sagent Lending Technologies entered an agreement to acquire fellow mortgage servicing technology provider ISGN Corp. for an undisclosed sum. Melbourne, Fla.-based ISGN was founded in 2007 and has raised funding from New Enterprise Associates and KK Birla Group.

New Money

Elysium Health Inc., a New York-based dietary supplement maker, raised $40 million in Series C equity and debt funding. GISEV Family Office led the round, which included participation from General Catalyst, Morningside Ventures, Breyer Capital, Mayo Clinic Ventures and Western Technology Investment.

Snackpass, a San Francisco-based restaurant food-ordering app, picked up $21 million in Series A funding. Andreessen Horowitz led the round, with participation from First Round Capital, General Catalyst, YCombinator, Inspired Capital and others.

Copilot, a New York- and Tel Aviv-based automated customer experience platform, secured a $5 million investment from Jerusalem Venture Partners.

Tech News

* Walmart, Target embrace 8-year-old YouTube influencer's brand.

* Drugmakers are trying to win drug approvals by parsing vast data sets of electronic medical records, shifting away from lengthy and costly clinical trials in patients.

WeWork: The Enablers

Adam Neumann, WeWork's former CEO, has been under intense scrutiny since the company's fall from grace. But there's also another group of people behind the dramatic unraveling: WeWork's investors. The WSJ's Maureen Farrell and Eliot Brown take us into the thinking of WeWork's biggest backers.

WSJ Pro
Corporations Notched Gains From AI in 2019, Executives Say; Though the impact is hard to quantify, tech leaders say AI efforts add business value

By Jared Council 
611 words
24 December 2019
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Corrections & Amplifications

Mojgan Lefebvre is chief technology and operations officer of Travelers Cos. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated her title as chief information officer. (Dec. 24, 2019)

Companies ramped up their artificial-intelligence projects in 2019 in an effort to better manage operations and lower risks, and many technology executives said they expect that drive to continue or accelerate in 2020.

Although it can be difficult to measure the impact on the bottom line, the executives said they believe their AI efforts added business value. The insights came from 30 technology leaders who participated in The Wall Street Journal's annual end-of-year questionnaire, which asked them about their technology budgets, among other issues.

Travelers Cos. turned to AI this fall to help speed up the claims process related to California wildfires. The insurer developed an image-recognition system that assesses property damage caused by the blazes, enabling the company to start paying claims before manually reviewing geospatial images or sending human inspectors. The system analyzes thousands of images and identifies with more than 98% accuracy which properties can be considered a total loss.

"Early analysis shows that using this unique solution will enable us to issue advance payments on about 90% of wildfire total loss claims prior to having an in-person inspection," said Travelers Chief Technology and Operations Officer Mojgan Lefebvre.

The average number of active AI projects at companies is expected to grow to 10 next year from four this year, according to a survey by research and advisory firm Gartner Inc. By 2022, the number of AI projects is set to increase to 35, Gartner predicts. The firm surveyed 106 respondents for the 2019 report; half of the participants represented companies with annual revenue of $1 billion to $3 billion.

JPMorgan Chase & Co. said in the WSJ questionnaire that it is using AI to reduce fraud losses. The bank developed a machine-learning system that approves or denies transactions for 40 million credit-card accounts. The bank is also using AI to spot-check fraud by analyzing signatures, payee names and check features in real time.

CIO Lori Beer said the fraud applications are expected to save the bank about $150 million a year.

Other AI systems that technology leaders are using are designed to streamline back-office tasks, such as applications that can predict future demand and inventory needs.

Target Corp. is using a machine-learning system for forecasting and inventory management. The system analyzes sales trends, inventory levels and other factors to determine how much of an item should be in a store's inventory.

The system was built in house by the retailer's data-science team, said CIO Mike McNamara. Although the company didn't provide specific numbers, Mr. McNamara said the system was "expanded vastly in 2019 and is now actively managing a good percentage of our store assortment." The move has helped improve on-shelf availability, he added.

In addition to streamlining operations, some businesses are using AI to better manage their human resources. Schneider Electric SE, a France-based industrial company, this year deployed a platform that uses AI to pair employees with career opportunities, including projects and full-time positions. The system identifies open opportunities within the company for employees based on their skills, experience and ambitions.

"While AI value has been limited so far, we are optimistic about future benefits," said Schneider CIO Elizabeth Hackenson.

SEC Investigates Listings of Slack, Others; Small Biotech M&As May Precede Big Buyouts; TikTok's HQ Hunt

By Tomio Geron

14:59
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. The direct listings of Slack and Spotify were supposed to portend a new way for venture-backed companies to go public. Some venture capitalists such as Benchmark's Bill Gurley have argued that the direct-listing process is much better than traditional IPOs.

But now the SEC is investigating the direct listings of Slack and other companies, as well as other IPOs, The Wall Street Journal reports. While the investigation is in its early stages, it could pump the brakes on the potential direct listings of other companies that had been expected in 2020.

Meanwhile, biotech acquisitions have been relatively paltry in 2019, due to the strength of the IPO market and the consolidation of large acquirers. But some investors remain optimistic for 2020, citing the patent expirations of drug manufacturers and other factors that could spark deal-making in the new year.

And now on to the news...

Top News

Biotech M&A. Biotechnology venture capitalists are hoping a recent flurry of acquisitions of small drugmakers portends a larger slate of buyouts coming up in 2020, WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley reports.

* Biotech startup acquisitions sputtered through the first three quarters of this year, with only 20 venture-backed companies purchased, the fewest since the same number were acquired over the same period in 2012, according to VentureSource.

* When the final 2019 tally of drug-startup acquisitions comes in, it will likely be well below the 42 in 2017 and last year's 37. But a run of buyouts of privately held and smaller publicly traded biotechs of late is an encouraging sign.

* Despite the consolidation of large potential pharmaceutical company acquirers and a strong IPO market that could limit acquisition activity, a new class of buyers and creative strategies such as options deals could help fuel more and larger exits in 2020, investors say.

* Many drug manufacturers need new products because their portfolios are maturing and patents for their top medications are nearing expiration, challenges that mergers and acquisitions can address, said Brian Abrahams, an analyst with RBC Capital Markets.

Direct-listing drama. The Securities and Exchange Commission is investigating the listings of Slack and other major companies on the NYSE, in a probe looking at how trading was handled on the first day, the Journal reported, citing people familiar with the situation. SEC enforcement staff have recently sent letters including one seeking information from electronic-trading firm Citadel Securities LLC related to how it opened Slack's stock for trading on June 20 in the workplace-messaging app's so-called direct listing, the people said. It also seeks information on other IPOs.

HQ hunt. Bytedance Inc. is considering setting up a global headquarters for its hit video-sharing app TikTok outside of China, part of continuing efforts to shake off its Chinese image, the Journal reported, citing people familiar with the company. Singapore is one city being considered, the people said. Other possible locations include London and Dublin, with no American cities on the shortlist, one person said.

"The direct listing of Slack was a tremendous success for the company, its shareholders and our nation's capital markets. From our vantage point, we stand firmly behind the integrity and transparency of the listing and pricing process on this important transaction."

 Citadel Securities

Industry News

Funds

Red & Blue Ventures has raised $9.5 million toward a $20 million second fund, according to a regulatory filing. The Philadelphia-based firm makes seed- and early-stage investments in companies from the University of Pennsylvania ecosystem.

Exits

The Lexington, Mass.-based company was backed by investors including Flagship Pioneering, Polaris Partners, RA Capital Management, Yonghua Capital and Norma Investments. Taris will become part of Janssen R&D, and will keep a research presence in the Lexington area.

"A lot of times these [biotech] companies get exciting valuations to go public and they have to weigh that against entering into an M&A transaction."

Mercato Partners led the investment, with contributions from Founders Fund, Lewis and Clark Ventures, Revolution's Rise of the Rest Seed Fund, Dundee Venture Capital and Victress Capital.

 Machine learning : the big risks and how to manage them

Mark Yallop 
804 words
23 December 2019
10:23
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

There is no precedent for how this type of trading, which adapts based on experience, might play out

Algorithmic trading has been prevalent in equities trading for more than two decades. It is also now well entrenched in fixed income, too.

It has created new opportunities by speeding up execution of orders, cutting costs and increasing volumes. But it has also introduced new hazards for market participants and created the occasional  flash crash . We have also seen trading algorithms being programmed to manipulate markets.

Now we face an even bigger challenge   machine learning. This technology, centred on computer models that can learn from experience, poses serious challenges and requires a global response.

The Bank of England and the UK Financial Regulation Authority recently published a survey of banks and capital markets firms which found that about half of respondents use machine learning in a modest way today. Most expect to make much greater use of it over the next few years.

Today, machine learning is deployed mainly in back-office functions such as anti-money laundering, fraud detection and credit risk management. It is not currently used much in front-line trading functions, but we expect material change in this area over the next few years.We therefore have the responsibility to consider potential risks and to mitigate them, as far as possible.

Unlike traditional rules-based algorithms, machine-learning algorithms are not static engines, programmed to run only along the paths created by their human programmers.

Instead, they use massive data sets and enormous computational power to enable them to recognise patterns, train themselves, and to make decisions about when and how to trade without human intervention.

This is a transformative moment for those trading in financial markets. It will bring great opportunities, but it will also create new hazards that we simply have not had to think about before. Here are four to consider.

First, what we call  model drift . Machine-learning trading engines learn for themselves how to create prices by repeated and constantly evolving experimentation. In this optimisation process it becomes hard, or even impossible, to trace how decisions are made. It is therefore very difficult to prevent undesirable outcomes in advance, or to correct them afterwards.

These concerns over transparency explain the present regulatory focus on model risk management and software validation, as well as questions about how company boards can satisfy themselves that they have an appropriate level of understanding about what is going on inside the  black box .

Second, bias. Machine learning creates the potential for unexpected or unfair changes in pricing or liquidity for certain types of market users, or even for individual customers   as a result of factors that are impossible to uncover because they lie, effectively undiscoverable, in the heart of the optimisation engine. Unless the machine chooses to tell you its secrets, you will never know why it did something.

More worrying, perhaps, is that a machine optimising on its own will probably find that unethical, manipulative trading practices are more profitable. How do we ensure that the machine understands not just the law and regulatory rules, but also concepts of right and wrong?

Third, market concentration. The way in which machine learning models improve by accessing increasing quantities of data is likely to create network effects, where a small number of data providers effectively control access. That may, in turn, throw up high barriers to entry.

Such barriers could entrench the power of today s large banks and financial services firms or, alternatively, allow technology-based competitors to create new oligopolies at the expense of today s financial sector.

But the consequences of concentrated market structures need careful thought. Participants in the market could be disadvantaged, through unfair rationing of liquidity and skewed pricing.

Fourth, the skills gap. There is a massive shortfall of expert programmers, data scientists and risk managers needed to safely develop, test and implement machine learning in financial markets.

This is just as much the case in the private sector as it is among central banks and market regulators. It creates a significant knowledge gap in the boardrooms of financial services firms and within policymaking institutions about the challenges and hazards posed by machine learning.

Given the international nature of financial markets, these are all challenges that need to be properly considered and addressed at a global level.

The complexity of the issues raised also makes collaboration between public authorities and the private sector essential. A fragmented approach could lead to a trading environment where no one truly knows what the black box is going to do. That is a risk for everyone, not just wholesale markets in one location or another.

The writer is chairman of the Fixed Income, Currencies and Commodities Markets Standards Board

C Suite
Cloud and Serverless Computing: Tech's Unsung Heroes; On the other hand, some CIOs say quantum computing, AI and blockchain were overhyped in 2019

By Sara Castellanos 
792 words
23 December 2019
14:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Artificial intelligence, fifth-generation wireless networks and blockchain were overhyped in 2019, while cloud and serverless computing deserved more attention than they got, some information-technology executives said.

Investments in AI, 5G and blockchain will need to undergo extra levels of scrutiny as a result of the hype, said Manjit Singh, group vice president and chief information officer of Toyota Motor Corp.'s North American unit. "These technologies will mature significantly over the next few years, but will have less impact as people tire of unrealistic expectations that have already been set," he said.

Mr. Singh was one of 30 IT executives who responded via email to CIO Journal's annual end-of-year questionnaire about their thoughts on emerging technologies and other issues.

Going serverless

One important technology that is adding business value but hasn't generated much buzz is serverless computing, said Nigel Faulkner, head of technology at financial-services company T. Rowe Price Group Inc.

In this emerging method of software development, physical and virtual servers traditionally used to run certain applications "https://blogs.wsj.com/cio/2016/12/15/serverless-computing-heats-up-as-pivotal-software-takes-on-cloud-giants/" because the servers are owned and managed by a cloud provider. This frees developers from having to manage servers and enables them to focus on writing code.

Serverless computing can modernize applications while reducing the costs necessary to run and support them, Mr. Faulkner said.

Dick Daniels, chief information officer at hospital and health-insurance company Kaiser Permanente, said cloud computing overall deserves more attention. "Cloud will be a key enabler for Kaiser Permanente to provide new capabilities faster, with greater mobility and flexibility and potentially at a lower cost," he said.

Nothing artificial about AI hype

Contributing to the hype surrounding AI is so-called AI washing, which refers to companies that say they are using AI when they are not.

More End-of-Year Coverage

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/employers-move-to-attract-tech-talent-before-graduation-11576850400"

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/businesses-keep-a-lid-on-number-of-cybersecurity-vendors-11577097002"

Technologies including AI should be used only to serve a specific purpose for the business, said Bob Worrall, senior vice president and CIO at networking and cybersecurity firm Juniper Networks Inc. "Any new technology is just a means to an end, so without first identifying what that end looks like, the rest of the discussion is moot," he said.

The term "artificial intelligence" will soon become meaningless, in part because it's already embedded across many areas, said Colleen Berube, CIO of software company Zendesk Inc.

Welcome back, blockchain

Blockchain was significantly overhyped in 2019 "https://blogs.wsj.com/cio/2018/12/28/expect-more-of-the-same-for-blockchain-in-2019-say-cios/", 2017, etc. many IT leaders said. The technology has the potential to secure transactions, to increase transparency and efficiency of data-sharing and to establish provenance, yet some IT leaders said they are unconvinced.

"While the proposition of an immutable distributed ledger seems obvious, I am not aware of a widely successful production deployment," said Ashim Gupta, interim chief information officer at automation company UiPath Inc.

Blockchain has failed to have the level of impact in the financial-services industry that many predicted, said Mr. Faulkner of T. Rowe Price.

Robot takeover

Several executives said robotic process automation, in which software "robots" automate mundane and low-level tasks, got more attention than it deserved this year. "RPA does not solve every problem, yet it's constantly being posed as the solution," said Vipul Nagrath, CIO at Automatic Data Processing Inc. Software robots are "simply today's latest version of automation, which has existed for decades," he said.

Hopes for 5G

Some IT leaders said 5G got too much attention last year and didn't result in many tangible business benefits, but at least one CIO is bullish on its potential. "I'm personally very excited about the innovation that 5G will bring and believe it will be one of the biggest game-changers we've seen in the history of health care," said Mark Boxer, CIO of health insurer Cigna Corp.

A quantum of hype

Some executives said quantum computing was overhyped this year, mostly because it will still be several years before the next-generation technology can generate useful business applications.

Early-stage quantum computers are being built and tested by technology giants including Microsoft Corp. and Amazon.com Inc.'s Amazon Web Services.

"I was in fourth grade when I heard about nuclear fusion and that was 10-20 years away. It's been 35 years and we're nowhere close that's how I see quantum computing," said Vijay Sankaran, CIO at TD Ameritrade Holding Corp.

Today's Top Stories

Largest Harvest Fund: Harvest Partners has gathered at least $4 billion for its eighth midmarket fund, making the vehicle the largest the firm has raised to date. Goldman Sachs Wipeout: Natural gas producer Arsenal Energy Holdings LLC, whose backers include Goldman Sachs Group Inc.'s private-equity arm, won bankruptcy court approval for its second prepackaged restructuring this year, cutting roughly $400 million in debt and turning creditors into shareholders. The deal received the court's blessing Thursday, WSJ Pro Bankruptcy's Aisha Al-Muslim reports. The restructuring will wipe out the equity stakes of the Goldman Sachs unit, First Reserve Management LP and Northwestern Mutual Life Insurance Co.

Leveraged Financing Caution Flag: The booming market for leveraged loans, a favorite source of buyout financing, has created vulnerabilities in the global financial system, according to a report from international regulators. The Financial Stability Board said a limited number of big banks are most exposed to these markets because they arrange the loans as well as underwrite the creation of structured investment funds known as collateralized loan obligations, The Wall Street Journal's Paul J. Davies reports.

Prying Open Private Markets: More Americans would be able to reap the rewards of investing early in the next Uber Technologies Inc. or Facebook Inc. under a proposal advanced Wednesday by the Securities and Exchange Commission. They could also get more chances to lose their shirts, The Wall Street Journal's Paul Kiernan reports. The proposal would expand the number of people allowed to invest in private-equity funds by allowing investors with certain qualifications, such as an entry-level stockbroker's license, to sidestep current income and wealth thresholds.

Growth-equity growth. The latest firm to jump on the growth-equity trend is Volition Capital, which raised $600 million for a pair of such funds, continuing an investment strategy that has attracted greater interest from venture and buyout firms, WSJ Pro's Yuliya Chernova reports.

News
Singapore steps up investment in medtech, energy and AI start-ups

HENNY SENDER 
749 words
19 December 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
15
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Financials. Strategic focus

City-state is in position as the value-added heart and brain of the region while HK flounders

Akshay Garg raised $90m for his Indonesian fintech start-up FinAccel earlier this month, from investors including the Korean group Mirae.

FinAccel has a million customers using its Kredivo service, which extends credit to shoppers. Mr Garg credits Singapore, where its holding group is, for making it easy for him to access capital.

"The entire south-east Asia [tech] ecosystem would not exist without Singapore," he said. "Singapore Inc is critical. We are only here because of Singapore. Without the connection to Singapore, we couldn't have gotten money from South Korea."

Several other Indonesian start-ups and their investors have benefited from relationships with the Singaporean government, either receiving grants or investment from its financial arms or from state-linked groups, or getting advice and connections from a network of south-east Asian tech start-ups.

Singapore is using its financial clout and $275bn in foreign reserves not including the sums managed by wealth fund GIC and investment group Temasek or funds that can be deployed by state-linked groups such as Singtel, an investor in FinAccel to upgrade its economy strategically.

A generation ago, it focused on highvalue industries such as pharma. Today it is investing in medtech, energy and artificial intelligence start-ups in the hope of reducing reliance on trade and boosting the skills of its labour force.

It is positioning itself as the valueadded heart and brain of the region, investing alongside strategic participants such as Google or venture capital firm Sequoia.

Singapore's activist approach contrasts with that of Hong Kong. "Singapore uses its money as the catalyst for change," said Shaun Lim, a Singaporean based in Hong Kong.

Mr Lim manages a joint venture tech fund between Beijing-based Hopu Investment Management and SoftBankcontrolled UK semiconductor firm Arm Holdings.

Pavilion, a unit of Temasek, is an investor in the fund and in some of its portfolio groups such as Appier, a Taipei-based AI marketing start-up.

His boss, Beijing dealmaker Fang Feng Lei, regularly meets Ho Ching, boss of Temasek.

"The Singaporeans think strategically. Their allocation is far more passive. They have less of a sense of urgency," Mr Lim said.

Hong Kong is less transparent about recipients of its money, which include most of the big US buyout groups such as Blackstone, and the fees it pays them.

Anyone logging on to the website of the Singapore Economic Development Board's investment arm, EDB-I, can find a list of the companies in which it has invested or from which it has already exited.

"We help Singapore and Singapore companies generate growth by nurturing new businesses," said Paul Ng, a senior vice-president at EDB-I. "We want to make all SMEs AI-enabled. We want to improve the environment and develop talent."

For many tech start-ups in the region, the EDB and EDB-I are the first stop.

"The EDB built Singapore," said Willson Cuaca, whose East Ventures invests in the earliest rounds of mostly Indonesian companies, including Tokopedia and Traveloka.

Singapore has invested in East Ventures, but only in its fifth fund, after it had established a record.

"They take risk, but they have also positioned themselves as a haven," he said.

Credo Biomedical, which has pioneered a cheap, speedy test for influenza, for example, is working with both EDB and Enterprise Singapore. The firm was founded by Wilson Wang, who is of the third generation of the family that founded Formosa Plastics in Taiwan. He holds Singaporean citizenship.

"On the financial front, the healthcare and biomedical team of Enterprise Singapore looked at our case and guided us patiently on the various financial grants we can apply," said Tan Tai Lim of Credo.

The group applied for a grant which will reimburse Credo for up to 70 per cent of eligible costs to take part in a medical convention in Dubai.

The EDB's investment arm has invested in start-ups outside the region to bring in knowledge on diseases from diabetes to eye maladies.

While outsiders sometimes wonder how to differentiate between the many arms of Singapore, especially as they evolve, to insiders it is a club in which everyone supports a national mandate.

Many in Singapore liken the country to Israel. "They believe they have no fallback," said one observer.

Sapphire Ventures Bulks Up Late-Stage Investing War Chest; SAP spinout raised roughly $1.4 billion in capital to target mature startups

By Marc Vartabedian 
480 words
18 December 2019
13:00
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Sapphire Ventures has raised roughly $1.4 billion it intends to invest in late-stage startups, adding to a growing pool of capital that tech startups are drawing upon to stay private longer.

Sapphire will use the infusion to double down on investing in enterprise software, with a special focus on startups that incorporate artificial intelligence, said Chief Executive Officer and Managing Director Nino Marakovic.

The bulk of the capital will go toward a new growth-stage fund and a new opportunity fund, Mr. Marakovic said.

The growth stage fund has roughly $1 billion and the opportunity fund has $150 million, according to regulatory filings. The balance of the $1.4 billion will top off existing funds.

Sapphire has also pursued late-stage investments with "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sap-spinoff-sapphire-ventures-raises-1-billion-1473879930" in 2016 after the firm was spun out of SAP SE in 2011. The German business software maker is Sapphire's largest limited partner.

The $1.4 billion brings Palo Alto, Calif.-based Sapphire's total assets under management to roughly $4 billion and adds to the growing availability of growth-stage financing, or investments for mature startups, in recent years.

Through the third quarter of this year, venture firms raised roughly $9.7 billion of late-stage capital, down from $10.8 billion through the same period last year but up from $5.9 billion for all of 2017, according to VentureSource, a data provider owned by WSJ Pro Venture Capital publisher Dow Jones & Co.

Startup investors are "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-shareholders-criticize-company-after-wework-wipeout-11574764205" for large late-stage financing deals--particularly those that "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-money-men-who-enabled-adam-neumann-and-the-wework-debacle-11576299616"We Co.'s WeWork derailed initial public offering and several large valuation "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-books-loss-of-4-7-billion-on-wework-investment-11573024776" of startups backed by outsized rounds from SoftBank Group Corp.

With the new capital, Sapphire will invest up to $100 million per company, Mr. Marakovic said. The firm's two new funds will target startups in the U.S., Europe and Israel and will allow Sapphire to invest in startups in Series B to pre-IPO stages.

"We're bulking up to be able to lead growth rounds. But we believe too large isn't good either," Mr. Marakovic said, adding that there is a difference between helping companies prepare for exits and showering startups with capital.

Sapphire's new funds come less than a year "https://www.wsj.com/articles/manchester-city-footballs-parent-heads-investor-list-backing-new-sapphire-sports-tech-fund-11548676801"Sapphire Sport Parallel Fund LP, a $115 million fund that targets early-stage technology companies in the sports, media and entertainment industries with investments of up to $4 million.

Sapphire also invests in early-stage venture funds, though the two new funds won't be used for that, said Scott Brown, the firm's vice president of marketing.

Good day. Hims & Hers was once known for its provocative and racy ads, but now the startup is making an effort to join the formal health-care system with a new partnership with a large health organization and a new funding round that may top $200 million.

In the News

Beyond racy ads. Telemedicine startup Hims & Hers, known for its suggestive ads for erectile-dysfunction treatment, has always wanted to be part of the formal health-care system. Now it wants to put that plan into action, WSJ Pro's Heather Mack reports exclusively.

* Unlike most digital health startups, which typically partner with established health organizations from the outset, San Francisco-based Hims & Hers went direct to consumers in 2017 when it began offering virtual doctor visits and prescription drug services for embarrassing conditions such as ED and hair loss.

* Two years on, in an effort to be seen less like a consumer brand and more like a legitimate part of the traditional health-care system, the company is offering treatment for more conditions, and has formed its first partnership with a large health organization.

* It remains to be seen whether Hims & Hers can achieve that goal while holding on to its reputation as a way of bypassing the traditional health-care system to get treatment.

Apartment hotels. Venture capitalists still see prospects in real estate, but are shifting their focus to property-rental startups that build on the Airbnb Inc. model, WSJ Pro's Katie Roof reports. Consumers flock to Airbnb for apartment listings, but they are increasingly interested in professionally managed properties, with fresh towels and stylish furniture, investors say. The latest such startup to reveal it has raised funding is Domio Inc., which provides apartment hotel-style accommodations in several cities nationwide. It raised $50 million in an August investment led by GGV Capital.

[Nino Marakovic, Sapphire Ventures CEO]

New funds. Venture firm Sapphire Ventures has raised roughly $1.4 billion it intends to invest in late-stage startups, adding to a growing pool of capital that tech startups are drawing upon to stay private longer, WSJ Pro's Marc Vartabedian reports. Sapphire will use the infusion to double down on investing in enterprise software, with a focus on startups that incorporate artificial intelligence. The bulk of the capital will go to a new growth-stage fund, and a new opportunity fund.

Growth deal. Health-insurance startup Bright Health Inc. said it has raised $635 million in a venture financing led by New Enterprise Associates that pushes its total equity funding to more than $1 billion, WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley reports. Formed in late 2015, Bright Health is an insurance provider and health plan operator led by Chief Executive Bob Sheehy, the former chief executive of health insurer UnitedHealthcare Group.

Unicorns a little less magical. Thirteen U.S. "unicorns," or startups valued at $1 billion or more, made their public debuts in 2019 far more than in any prior year. Despite their growing numbers and valuations, the post-IPO performance of unicorns has been a mixed bag. On the whole, an investor in the second half of the decade was likelier to have put money into a unicorn that was unprofitable and whose value has dropped as a public company than an investor in the decade's first half, The Wall Street Journal found.

Private equity for adtech. Private-equity firm Providence Equity Partners LLC is taking a majority stake in venture-backed social video advertising technology provider Smartly.io Solutions Oy as the firm bets that brands will spend more marketing dollars on Facebook and other social platforms, the Journal's Sahil Patel reports for CMO Today.

Big Number

$8 Billion

Amount invested last year in 202 travel and leisure startups, according to VentureSource, a data provider owned by WSJ Pro publisher Dow Jones & Co.

Fresh Credit Suisse Spying Report Puts Surveillance Tactics in Focus; Swiss bank probes new revelation of alleged spying on a top executive

By Margot Patrick and Jenny Strasburg 
865 words
18 December 2019
18:26
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Credit Suisse Group AG is probing a third episode of alleged spying on a top executive, highlighting the ethical and reputational pitfalls companies encounter when they physically monitor employees.

The Swiss bank said it is conducting a review after a Swiss newspaper published a photo allegedly showing a top bank executive under surveillance earlier this year, a spokesman said.

The latest revelation follows a September clash on the streets of Zurich between another top Credit Suisse executive and a detective the bank hired to tail him. Separately, The Wall Street Journal reported this month that Colleen Graham, a former Credit Suisse executive who headed a joint venture for the bank, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/former-executive-at-credit-suisse-joint-venture-alleges-bank-spied-on-her-11576094110" by an investigator during an alleged three-day surveillance in July 2017. Credit Suisse said Ms. Graham's claim was baseless.

The Swiss media report, in the Neue Zrcher Zeitung newspaper this week, said Peter Goerke, a longtime lieutenant of Chief Executive Tidjane Thiam, was followed for three days in February, citing documents and photos. Shortly after the alleged spying, Credit Suisse removed Mr. Goerke from the bank's executive board. He gave up his job as head of human resources and became a senior adviser on bank projects. Mr. Goerke couldn't be reached for comment.

The alleged incidents involving Credit Suisse cast another shadow over Switzerland's second-largest lender by assets. Revenue at its investment bank plunged this year as corporate deals failed to materialize, forcing the bank to cut its financial targets last week. Credit Suisse's share price is down by more than half since Mr. Thiam started as CEO in July 2015.

In September, former wealth management head Iqbal Khan's confrontation with a bank-hired detective sparked weeks of Swiss tabloid headlines and global news reports.

The bank hired a law firm, Homburger AG, to conduct a review into Mr. Khan's surveillance. The law firm found it was ordered by Chief Operating Officer Pierre-Olivier Boue, another close lieutenant of Mr. Thiam. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/credit-suisse-coo-resigns-over-surveillance-scandal-11569908569" and hasn't commented on the law firm's findings. The Homburger partner who conducted the review referred a Wednesday request for comment to Credit Suisse.

In a memo to staff after the incident involving Mr. Khan, Mr. Thiam said the surveillance was "strictly an isolated incident."

Credit Suisse said that a previous review of surveillance on another executive hadn't identified evidence of Mr. Goerke being shadowed.

The line between company security and employee privacy is often blurry. Physical surveillance is typically a last resort for companies including banks, even when they suspect employees of serious ethical or legal breaches such as fraud, bribery or other behavior that violates company rules, experts say.

One reason: Covert surveillance can backfire if it fails to gather the desired evidence and is discovered by the target and the public, as has happened at Credit Suisse.

"Surveillance might just produce circumstantial evidence," said Andrew Watson, a London employment lawyer who represents both companies and employees and spoke about general practices, not Credit Suisse specifically.

If spying is bungled, Mr. Watson said, "You look like you're suspicious of your employees. That has repercussions, in ways that are really hard to quantify, about how people feel about going to work."

Steps taken to spy on employees should be "justified and proportionate" to any suspected wrongdoing, said Tristan Prince of NetWatch Global Ltd., a U.K. corporate-intelligence firm that does online investigations and digital surveillance for corporate clients.

"You usually wouldn't start by putting [physical] surveillance on employees right away," Mr. Prince said. Usually, "you have to have some kind of indication there's fraud, collusion, money laundering" or a similar violation.

Ultimately, experts said, so much information is online and available to banks including about employees that technology provides easier, lower-risk investigative tools. More broadly, banks are already heavily security-focused and more sophisticated than ever about how they gather data on customers, competitors and their own employees, experts say.

"Every company is monitoring employees in some fashion. It would be naive to think otherwise," said Brian Vecci of software and analytics company Varonis Systems Inc., which helps banks and other companies protect data. "The question is to what extent, and what's appropriate."

"I use my company phone for work, but my company doesn't have access to my personal email and texting, Mr. Vecci said. "I would probably have a problem with that."

Still, several people who have seen bank employee surveillance in action said a lot of people would be surprised how much of their behavior can be monitored. Banks have access to executives' personal financial accounts if they're held at their employer, for example. One of the people said they have seen a bank look into employees' personal accounts to investigate suspicions of wrongdoing such as bribery or an inappropriate relationship.

Write to Margot Patrick at "mailto:margot.patrick@wsj.com" and Jenny Strasburg at "mailto:jenny.strasburg@wsj.com"

Good day. Intel has acquired Habana Labs, an Israel-based startup, for approximately $2 billion. The company had raised $120 million from investors, according to PitchBook Data.

Plus, space startup Vector Launch filed for bankruptcy, blaming investor Sequoia Capital for not putting up more cash.

Meanwhile, Flagship Pioneering has hired a former Bristol-Myers Squibb executive to work with its startups to develop new drugs.

And Andreessen Horowitz has hired Kristina Shen, previously of Bessemer Venture Partners, as its newest general partner.

And now on to the news...

In the News

AI exit. Chip-making giant Intel is expanding its push into the artificial-intelligence market with an approximately $2 billion deal for Habana Labs, an Israel-based AI chip startup, the Journal's Asa Fitch and Michael Dabaie report.

* Habana had raised $120 million from investors including Intel Capital, Battery Ventures, Samsung Catalyst, Bessemer Venture Partners and Walden Venture Capital, according to PitchBook Data.

* Intel said the deal strengthens its lineup of artificial-intelligence chips for use in data centers, the large facilities where companies are running AI algorithms to analyze data to, for instance, identify customer trends that otherwise might be too hard to spot.

* Even before announcing the Habana purchase, Intel said it expected to generate more than $3.5 billion in AI-related sales this year, up around 20% from last year.

* The Habana deal isn't Intel's first acquisition to bolster its lineup of AI products. Intel bought startup Nervana Systems in 2016 for around $400 million and followed that by purchasing Irish startup Movidius.

Space crash. Space-technology company Vector Launch Inc. has filed for bankruptcy, blaming its demise on venture-capital backer Sequoia Capital's decision not to provide additional funding for its development efforts, WSJ Pro's Alexander Gladstone reports. Vector, along with its subsidiary Garvey Spacecraft Corp., filed for chapter 11 protection in U.S. Bankruptcy Court in Wilmington, Del., with a deal to sell its GalacticSky satellite software business to Lockheed Martin for $4.25 million. Vector had raised about $100 million in venture capital, with its Series A funding round led by Sequoia.

Drug development. Flagship Pioneering has hired the former business-development chief of pharmaceutical company Bristol-Myers Squibb Co. to lead an effort to help its biotechnology portfolio companies expand into working on new drugs they weren't planning to develop on their own, WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley reports. Flagship launches health-care and sustainability companies and has been looking to capture more value from its companies as they mature.

Imitating Amazon. The dominance by Amazon over digital retail sales has spawned a fast-growing ecosystem of startups and services aimed at matching different parts of Amazon's sprawling network and at helping retailers and brands of all sizes meet consumer expectations set by the e-commerce heavyweight, Jennifer Smith writes for the WSJ's Logistics Report. Taken together, the businesses are creating what amounts to a virtual logistics system in Amazon's shadow.

Gormley's Take: Drug Manufacturers Snap Up Gene-Therapy Companies

By Brian Gormley

Drug manufacturers are in the market for gene-therapy companies, propelled by concerns about competition for their products and looking to acquire novel medicines that could treat or cure diseases by compensating for faulty genes that cause illnesses.

Earlier this month, Japanese pharmaceutical company Astellas Pharma agreed to buy Audentes Therapeutics for $3 billion to gain a toehold in gene therapy and get the San Francisco-based company's manufacturing capabilities.

Audentes, a venture-backed company that went public in 2016, is developing a potential treatment for the rare neuromuscular disease x-linked myotubular myopathy.

The deal followed an agreement in February by Philadelphia-based Spark Therapeutics, which went public in 2015, to be acquired by Roche Holding. That same month, Sarepta Therapeutics exercised an option to acquire privately held, venture-backed Myonexus Therapeutics of New Albany, Ohio.

Read More in Gormley's Take.

PE Daily: Partners Plugs $2.2 Billion Into EyeCare | Bain Capital Fund's Debut | Oria Doubles Down on Zenvia

1,639 words
17 December 2019
14:37
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day! As we approach the shortest day of the year in the northern hemisphere, the pace of holiday shopping grows more frantic, and sometimes it seems that news flow follows suit. Likely this stems from principals trying to clear the decks before settling down for a long winter break. So today we have several stories to start you off, from Laura Cooper's look at a $2.2 billion Partners Group deal to her Bain Capital Tech Opportunities report.

There's also news from Brazil, where Oria Capital is enlarging its investment in Zenvia, a customer communications service provider, as Luis Garcia reports. Finally, Laura has one more with her coverage of LLR Partners' investment in Geoforce. And there are more items from the U.S. and Europe to keep you informed as we head toward next week's festivities, so please read on...

Today's Top Stories

Partners Group Buys U.S. Eye-Care Company in $2.2 Billion Deal

Partners Group Holding AG said it is adding one of the largest U.S. optometry and ophthalmology services groups to its portfolio of health-care investments in a transaction that a person familiar with the matter said values the company at $2.2 billion including debt, Laura Cooper writes for WSJ Pro. The deal to acquire a majority stake in EyeCare Partners LLC marks the end of an investment by FFL Partners, which invested in the optical care provider in 2015.

Bain Capital's New Technology Fund Makes Maiden Investment

Bain Capital has clinched its first deal out of a new fund focused on midmarket technology. The firm has backed cloud computing training platform A Cloud Guru to help fund the company's acquisition of Linux Academy Inc., an open-source software training provider, according to ACG. The vehicle, Bain Capital Tech Opportunities Fund LP, is also trying to raise as much as $1 billion for its strategy of making control and late-stage growth investments in midmarket tech companies. The firm aims to wrap up fundraising in next year's first half, people familiar with the matter said.

Brazil's Oria Capital Pumps Up Its Zenvia Investment

Brazilian private-equity firm Oria Capital led a $54 million funding round for Zenvia Mobile Servios Digitais SA, five years after making its first bet on the provider of tools to facilitate customer communication for businesses. The So Paulo-based firm made its latest investment from its third fund, Oria Tech III, and limited partners also participated in the funding round as co-investors, Oria Managing Partner Piero Rosatelli told WSJ Pro Private Equity's Luis Garcia. Zenvia develops chatbots and other software that businesses use to communicate with customers through messaging services and social media, including WhatsApp and Facebook.

Big Number

8x

The average leverage of private equity-owned software companies as a multiple of debt to Ebitda at deal closing this year, a new high, according to Matthew Jones, senior credit officer at Moody's Investors Service. The mulitple has been rising since 2013.

Deals

LLR Partners Invests in Equipment-Tracking Company Geoforce

Private-equity firm LLR Partners, in what is expected to be its final deal of the year, has invested in Geoforce Inc., a provider of track-and-trace operational intelligence to industrial services companies, WSJ Pro Private Equity's Laura Cooper reports. Plano, Texas-based Geoforce helps companies keep track of items such as tanks, cargo containers, generators and vehicles with satellite-connected devices about the size of a hockey puck that transmit location and other data to remote monitors.

AEA Investors-backed 1-800 Contacts, an online retailer of contact lenses serving nearly 20 million consumers, is acquiring 6over6 Vision Ltd., which provides eyeglasses to consumers through online and mobile apps. The Draper, Utah-based acquirer expects to close the transaction by the end of this month. Tel Aviv-based 6over6 has been backed by venture funds including Rimonci Capital, which has a fund dedicated to vision-focused enterprises, Triventures and Alumot Fund, according to Crunchbase. The company has developed methods to help consumers determine their corrective lens needs and order glasses online.

Private-equity firm Ardian has acquired professional sound-mixer supplier Audiotonix from fellow Paris-based investment firm Astorg, which will reinvest in the company alongside management and Ardian, the firms said. Astorg first acquired U.K.-based Audiotonix in 2017, buying a 58% stake from Epiris. Audiotonix sound mixers are used in recording studios and at major live events, including music festivals and concerts.

A Lone Star Funds-backed company in Spain is buying the tile coatings business of Ferro Corp. for as much as $492 million, Mayfield Heights, Ohio-based Ferro said Monday. Pigments Spain SL has agreed to pay $460 million in cash for the unit and could add $32 million more based on the performance of the business between now and when the deal closes. Pigments Spain is part of the Esmalglass-Itaca-Fritta group, based in Villarreal, Spain. The Ferro unit makes coatings for ceramic tiles used in residential and commercial construction and had $510 million in sales during the 12 months through September, Ferro said. Lone Star is located in Dallas.

Summit Partners has led a $90 million minority investment in Belgian business software provider Odoo alongside management and earlier investors, including SRIW and affiliate Noshaq as well as Sofinnova Partners and XAnge. The company was founded in 2005 and has more than 4.5 million users world-wide. Its products include software for accounting, customer management, human resources and project management.

J.D. Power, a data analytics provider, has been acquired by private-equity firm Thoma Bravo. The company will be combined with Thoma Bravo's Autodata Solutions, a provider of data and software for the automotive industry, and will operate under the J.D. Power name. Dave Habiger, president and chief executive of J.D. Power, will lead the combined company, which will be based in Troy, Mich. Craig Jennings, former president and CEO of Autodata Solutions, will be president of the Autodata division. In addition to providing information for the global automobile industry, J.D. Power will continue to provide benchmarks and analytics for other industries.

H.I.G. Growth Partners, the growth investment affiliate of Miami-based H.I.G. Capital, has participated in a $65 million Series D funding round for Passport Labs Inc., which operates a mobility management platform. The Charlotte, N.C.-based company serves nearly 1,000 clients world-wide, helping them solve mobility challenges, including parking, enforcement, permits and transit.

CallMiner Inc., a developer of speech-analytics software powered by artificial intelligence, has raised $75 million from the GS Growth investment unit of Goldman Sachs Group Inc., WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence's Jared Council reports. WSJ Pro
Cyber Daily: PNC and Venmo at Odds Over Security Protocols; Questions Arise on Foreign Parts in Voting Machines

741 words
17 December 2019
14:26
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. A skirmish between Venmo and PNC bank over security protocols means that some joint customers can't connect their accounts. Frustrated consumers have taken to Twitter, and each company blames the other.

Also today: New questions about foreign parts in U.S. voting machines; Iran says it knocked down two cyberattacks; and Navy warned about Chinese drones.

Supply-Chain Security

Venmo glitch opens window on war between banks, fintech firms. Many PNC bank clients are having trouble connecting their bank accounts to their Venmo apps, cutting off their access to the popular mobile-payment system, owned by PayPal Holdings Inc. When they have sought help, they have found the two companies blaming each other for the disruption and citing data security concerns, The Wall Street Journal reports.

Fintech companies say they need access to the customer-account information held by banks and other traditional financial companies. Banks and brokerage firms, protecting their own turf, have resisted.

At the center of the PNC-Venmo skirmish is Plaid, a data aggregator that connects Venmo and thousands of other apps to financial institutions.

Security concerns halt data-sharing. When PNC upgraded its security systems, it prevented Plaid from accessing customers' account and routing numbers. PNC began blocking data aggregators such as Plaid from gaining access to customers' account numbers and routing numbers after it identified "multiple different aggregators" attempting to circumvent the bank's security protocol, bank executives say.

"When aggregators access account numbers, many store them indefinitely, often unbeknownst to customers," said Karen Larrimer, head of retail banking and chief customer officer at PNC. "This puts customers and their money at risk," she said. "We want to make sure we know who is setting up the account."

Plaid says it had already worked with PNC to provide requested system updates. The bank says it has asked Plaid and other aggregators to make changes to their own systems to meet its security requirements.

Big Number

$5.2 Million

Size of an Energy Department program researching for ways to use artificial intelligence to protect critical infrastructure from cyberattacks. Baker Hughes, GE Research, Intel and Florida State University are collaborating with the agency on the three-year project due to start in 2020.

More Cyber News

Voting-machine parts made by foreign suppliers stir security concerns. A voting machine that is widely used across the U.S. contains some parts made by companies with ties to China and Russia, said a report by Interos Inc., an Arlington, Va.-based supply-chain monitoring company. The findings are likely to fan worries about whether voting-machine vendors are doing enough to defend themselves against foreign interference ahead of the 2020 U.S. elections, the WSJ reports.

Points of contention: Voting-machine vendors assailed the research, saying the report failed to note existing safeguards, such as testing done at the federal, state and local levels, and the vendors' internal protocols. The report examined one unnamed voting machine as a case study. In that machine, around 20% of the components in the supply chain that Interos was able to identify came from China-based companies, including processors, software and touch screens, according to the Interos research. Those components weren't necessarily made in China, as the suppliers may have several locations globally, and the Interos data doesn't necessarily cover the entire supply chain, the researchers noted. The Chinese embassy in Washington didn't immediately reply to a request for comment.

Iran said it stopped two cyberattacks. Attempts to attack government systems and electronic infrastructure in Iran were stopped by cybersecurity experts, according to the country's telecommunications minister. One of the incidents involved the APT27 malware, known to be used by Chinese-speaking hackers, the BBC reports. Few other details were released.

U.S. Navy concerned about data security on Chinese drones. Data collected and transmitted by drones from China-based DJI could be intercepted, according to a 2017 memo from the U.S. Navy obtained by George Washington University, CyberScoop reports. Techniques are available online to syphon data from the aerial vehicles, the Navy said. A DJI representative told CyberScoop that the company has fixed the vulnerabilities. A bipartisan group of lawmakers proposed a bill in September that would bar federal agencies from buying drones from China and any other country deemed a national-security risk.

Regulators Find Shortcomings in Resolution Plans at Six Large U.S. Banks; The banks must address the shortcomings by the end of March

By Andrew Ackerman 
432 words
17 December 2019
22:52
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON Regulators on Tuesday said they had found "shortcomings" in the resolution plans at six of the largest U.S. banks, while none of the eight major banks they examined had more serious "deficiencies."

The Federal Reserve and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. said the firms, including Bank of America Corp., Citigroup Inc. and Wells Fargo & Co. had shortcomings related to their ability to reliably produce data needed to execute orderly wind-downs, also known as living wills, "in stressed conditions."

The six banks, which also include Bank of New York Mellon Corp., Morgan Stanley and State Street Corp., must address the shortcomings by the end of March. They next submit living wills in 2021.

Tuesday's moves, the regulatory equivalent of a slap on the wrist, are less severe than the finding of a "deficiency," which could lead to more stringent capital and liquidity requirements for the firms.

The agencies didn't find shortcomings in the plans from Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and JPMorgan Chase & Co.

Fed officials have grown increasingly confident that big U.S. banks are safer than they were in 2008, when the financial crisis exposed significant weaknesses in their risk management.

Tuesday's findings are a turnabout from just three years ago, when the Fed ordered five big U.S. banks to make significant revisions to their plans, citing deficiencies.

"The largest banks have been making steady progress, and the regulators are in greater agreement on what progress is than in prior rounds," said Karen Petrou, managing partner of Federal Financial Analytics, a regulatory advisory firm.

Wells Fargo"https://newsroom.wf.com/press-release/corporate-and-financial/wells-fargo-issues-statement-2019-resolution-plan-feedback"the Fed found no deficiencies with its plan.

A Bank of New York spokeswoman said the lender "will work diligently to address the specific areas of feedback identified by the regulators in the required time frame."

Spokesmen for Bank of America, Citi and JPMorgan declined to comment. The other banks didn't immediately respond to requests for comment.

Kevin Fromer, president and chief executive of the Financial Services Forum, which represents the largest U.S. lenders, said the results show the firms "are strong, resilient and resolvable."

Under the 2010 Dodd-Frank law, big banks must file plans showing they have a credible strategy to go through bankruptcy without causing a broader economic panic, one of many provisions in the law designed to prevent a repeat of financial-crisis bailouts.

Banking & Finance: Goldman Unit Puts $75 Million in AI-Powered Firm

By Jared Council 
410 words
17 December 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B10
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

CallMiner Inc., a developer of speech-analytics software powered by artificial intelligence, said Monday that it raised $75 million in new venture funding from GS Growth, an investment unit of Goldman Sachs Group Inc.

The Waltham, Mass.-based company, which has about 250 employees, has raised a total of $142 million since it was founded in 2002.

CallMiner's software platform, called Eureka, uses AI to analyze conversations between customers and a company's representatives. Among other things, the platform can recommend what an agent should say and determine whether employees' answers comply with company protocols.

Eureka uses natural language processing and other techniques to understand the words and sentiment used in calls, web chats, emails, surveys and texts.

The deal marks the first investment in CallMiner by GS Growth, which is part of Goldman's merchant-banking division. CallMiner previously raised $67 million from investors including NewSpring Capital, Jackson Square Ventures and Sigma Prime Ventures. It has about 400 customers including Equifax Inc., Banco Santander SA, Sirius XM Holdings Inc. and Dell Technologies Inc.

Holger Staude, a managing director in Goldman's merchant-banking division, will join CallMiner's board. "CallMiner is well positioned to transform the way organizations operate," he said in a statement.

Jeff Gallino, CallMiner's founder and chief technology officer, said his company plans to use the money to enhance Eureka, including helping it make more prescriptive recommendations.

"I want it to be able to tell the enterprise, 'Hey, did you know when you have a caller on the line, and they mention this competitor and they're calling about product A, if you want to upsell them, here's how you do it,'" Mr. Gallino said.

Eureka has historically focused on contact-center operations, Mr. Gallino said, adding that the company wants to accelerate an expansion into other areas, such as marketing or customer experience, that could benefit from the analysis of customer interactions.

CallMiner competes against companies including NICE Ltd., Verint Systems Inc., and Genesys Telecommunications Laboratories Inc. in a category Forrester Research Inc. calls "AI-fueled speech analytics."

Enterprise demand for such technology has grown in recent years along with advances in deep-learning techniques, said Kjell Carlsson, a Forrester analyst.

Not only has the technology improved but companies are more interested than ever in improving customer experience, he added.

Startup Gecko Raises $40 Million for Robotic Infrastructure Inspection; The startup builds robots to inspect industrial equipment and prevent accidents

By Marc Vartabedian 
518 words
16 December 2019
11:00
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Gecko Robotics Inc., a startup providing robots to help industrial companies inspect infrastructure and prevent accidents, raised $40 million in venture funding.

The deal comes as more large industrial corporations are turning to artificial intelligence and robotics startups to leverage new technology and gain new business insights.

Gecko's robots and AI-based software help its customers, which include Duke Energy Corp., Royal Dutch Shell PLC and Chevron Corp., predict when and where infrastructure could fail.

Gecko's robots can climb tanks, boilers, pipes and other equipment and use lasers, cameras, electromagnetics and ultrasonics to capture data to assess the infrastructure's health.

Companies in a variety of sectors, including energy and agriculture, are exploring different types of robotics to help leverage new insights.

Gecko, an alum of startup accelerator Y Combinator based in Pittsburgh, will use the capital to hire staff, develop software and build more robots as it aims to expand into new sectors, according to co-founder and Chief Executive Jake Loosararian.

The Series B round was led by Drive Capital. Previous investors including Founders Fund, Hive Ventures and Y Combinator participated. In addition, Alpha VP and venture firm Next47 also participated. Last year Gecko "https://www.wsj.com/articles/gecko-robotics-collects-7-million-series-a-1534804196" in Series A funding led by Founders Fund, with Mark Cuban, the Westly Group, Justin Kan and Y Combinator participating.

To do inspections, Gecko's robots roll along a surface and use various methods to check for wall thickness, cracking and other forms of degradation.

The insights the robots collect can help reduce infrastructure failure, explosions, harmful emissions and billions of dollars of loss each year, Mr. Loosararian said. The robotic inspections, which reduce the need for human inspections, can also reduce human injury, Mr. Loosararian added.

Roughly 80% of Gecko's revenue is generated from customers in the energy sector, according to Mr. Loosararian, adding that a portion of the new capital will help Gecko's efforts to increase its business in the manufacturing and oil-and-gas industries.

Founded in 2013, Gecko has a fleet of about 50 robots and will use the capital in part to build another 50, Mr. Loosararian said. Gecko, which gets paid per job, will also use the capital to hire software and product engineers.

Large industrial companies are increasingly turning to startups building different types of robots to offer new insights. This month, for instance, drone startup PrecisionHawk Inc., "https://www.wsj.com/articles/drone-startup-nets-funding-as-regulatory-changes-spur-investor-interest-11576065601" and lists a variety of utility companies as customers that use its technology to help inspect their equipment.

And DroneDeploy Inc. last month "https://www.wsj.com/articles/drone-software-startup-raises-funding-for-business-insights-and-logistics-11573683541" and says that agricultural companies use its drone software to gather data and generate an array of information including crop moisture levels.

Hive Ventures partner Nina Achadjian says she is attracted to Gecko because it is introducing innovative technology to legacy industries.

"The [return on investment] is just very clear for these companies," Ms. Achadjian said.
