Goldman Taps Startup to Explore Quantum Computing; QC Ware to help bank determine whether the technology could speed up algorithm used to calculate theoretical value of an option

By Sara Castellanos 
635 words
10 December 2019
14:02
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Goldman Sachs Group Inc. has teamed up with a quantum-computing startup to explore how the nascent technology could be used to speed up financial calculations and artificial-intelligence-based decision making.

The bank worked with Palo Alto, Calif.-based startup QC Ware Corp. for three months earlier this year to understand the limitations of current quantum-computing technology, said Paul Burchard, a managing director at Goldman's research-and-development division.

Goldman is extending the partnership with QC Ware, Mr. Burchard said, adding, "We're looking at various possibilities for applying quantum computing across the bank." This is the first time Goldman has announced its work with a quantum-computing services company.

By harnessing the properties of quantum physics, quantum computers have the potential to sort through a vast number of possibilities in nearly real time and come up with a probable solution. While traditional computers store information as either zeros or ones, quantum computers use quantum bits, or qubits, which represent and store information as both zeros and ones simultaneously.

No commercial-grade quantum computer has been built yet, but tech giants such as Microsoft Corp. and Amazon.com Inc.'s Amazon Web Services"https://www.wsj.com/articles/amazon-rolls-out-quantum-computing-service-11575314729".

QC Ware uses the cloud to connect businesses with early-stage quantum-computing hardware providers including D-Wave Systems Inc., Rigetti Computing and International Business Machines Corp. The startup also helps enterprises develop quantum algorithms and applications.

Its other enterprise clients include Airbus SE, which "https://www.wsj.com/articles/airbus-cto-sees-quantum-computing-taking-off-in-aerospace-industry-11548255860" in understanding how quantum computing could be applied to mathematical calculations in the design phase of aircraft systems and parts.

In the coming months, QC Ware will help Goldman Sachs determine whether quantum computing could be used to speed up a computational algorithm known as Monte Carlo. The algorithm is used to calculate the theoretical value of an option, or a contract that gives individuals the right to buy or sell an underlying asset at a specific price and time.

These types of calculations can be time-consuming for traditional computers, which can take hours to compute option prices and risks for a large portfolio of complex trades.

Quantum-computing technology could also be useful in speeding up AI-based calculations that help determine trading strategies for clients, Mr. Burchard said. Theoretically, quantum computing has "some very attractive applications," he said. But today's quantum machines face numerous hardware challenges, meaning potential business value from the technology could still be many years away.

"We're obviously making a bet that it's worth it to get in now, early, and understand the technology sooner than later, but there's a lot of uncertainty around the eventual timeline," Mr. Burchard said.

QC Ware has received $7.5 million in venture-capital funding since it was founded in 2014. Goldman was the lead investor in the company's Series A funding round last year, along with Citigroup Inc.

A Goldman executive said last month that when quantum computing goes mainstream, the financial-services industry "https://www.wsj.com/articles/quantum-computing-holds-promise-for-banks-executives-say-11573230983" because a quantum algorithm could be deployed to a new financial model in days or weeks, while approving a new material or drug discovered by a quantum computer is likely to take years.

"In the universe of industries where there is a potential quantum advantage, you could argue that finance has got the shortest path to impact," Jeremy Glick, head of research-and-development engineering at Goldman Sachs, said at a quantum-computing panel event in Brooklyn, N.Y.

Write to Sara Castellanos at "mailto:sara.castellanos@wsj.com"

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge from fund managers/operators.

Investing in Funds & ETFs (A Special Report): Q&A --- Robert Shiller Talks About the Stories We Tell --- The economist explains how narratives affect the economy and markets; 'The overwhelming narrative is all about Donald Trump,' he says

By Dan Weil 
1,072 words
9 December 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
R8
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Yale University economist Robert Shiller has produced groundbreaking research about the economy and financial markets, and in 2013 shared a Nobel Prize with Eugene Fama and Lars Peter Hansen for work on analyzing asset prices. Mr. Shiller warned of a stock-market bubble before the 2001 market slide and a housing bubble before the plunge of 2008.

Now he is focusing on narratives, specifically the idea that popular stories, whether true or false, can affect the economy and markets. An example would be the false adage that home prices never fall.

Economists and investors ignore narratives at their peril, says Mr. Shiller, who recently published the book "Narrative Economics: How Stories Go Viral and Drive Major Economic Events."

The Wall Street Journal talked with Mr. Shiller about his research on narratives. Edited excerpts:

WSJ: Can you explain your idea about the role that narratives play in the economy and financial markets?

MR. SHILLER: It's important for investors and economists to look at what people think as things change through time. It can be done by studying their narratives. That is the telling of a story, which imbues it with something memorable.

People generally don't talk directly about their economic theories, but [they do reveal them] through stories. Investors are influenced and should know which stories are viral and whether they're being caught in those stories.

Years ago, fellow economist John Pound and I did a study about how people find investments. We concluded that most people aren't systematic. They aren't necessarily responding to tips, but they are responding to whatever is being talked about.

WSJ: What narratives do you think are at work now in the economy and financial markets?

MR. SHILLER: Technology is an important narrative. That [narrative] drove the dot-com boom and came back just before the financial crisis. It keeps mutating. Now it's artificial intelligence. That's a different emphasis than the earlier one about labor-saving machines.

It isn't scary at the moment. It isn't inhibiting consumer spending. But if the emphasis was on job reductions caused by robots, consumers might well cut back on spending. The fear could come back.

WSJ: What about narratives about the economy as a whole?

MR. SHILLER: The overwhelming narrative is all about Donald Trump. At the moment, [the Trump narrative] is amazingly strong. What does it do to economic activity and investors? It has the potential to scare people and make them pull back. But it hasn't so far. I don't have an answer as to why.

Donald Trump understands people have a success orientation. He helps to nourish that orientation. He encourages people to live high and show off. He thrusts forward the American dream in a new way. That's part of what's driving the economy, financial markets and the housing market.

WSJ: How do narratives play into recession?

MR. SHILLER: They are the dominant explanation for the strength of a recession. The Great Depression was great because of the narrative. Franklin Roosevelt said all we had to fear was fear itself. That phrase is remembered by many people. And it isn't just fear. Other emotions, like anger, might change the economy and markets.

WSJ: How can investors incorporate narratives into their thinking?

MR. SHILLER: An individual investor who has no particular expertise should just diversify. Maybe you can get a little edge by value investing. If you're more ambitious, and it appeals to you to think of these things, then go for it. You need to think about what's genuine value and what's imaginary, and then look at narratives to see what's driving a stock beyond earnings.

WSJ: So the goal is to figure out how narratives are contributing to an asset's price?

MR. SHILLER: Yes, you have to have a sense of the real business. Why does something have an anomalous price? Maybe it's a genuinely positive investment. But if so, why aren't investors driving it up? Maybe narratives are driving attention away from fundamentals.

Narratives can be a valuable part of value investing. I don't know how good Donald Trump is as an investor, but he's good at understanding narratives. He has a certain genius, though I certainly don't mean to praise everything he does. In any case, you can use narratives as part of successful investing if you have good human judgment.

WSJ: But that might be difficult for many investors -- trying to judge the truthfulness of narratives, right?

MR. SHILLER: Yes, it's kind of an art form. It's almost like being a historian. Maybe more people should be history majors, instead of business. My method is to go to digital sources and search for narratives that I see in the press, like the narratives surrounding artificial intelligence. I see how far back it goes and read the articles.

Sometimes a narrative lasts for decades. Others play out more quickly. You're trying to understand with human judgment whether you're reading real news or fake news. Narratives are inspired by real events. But sometimes they grow without being real themselves. In investing, it's even harder. You have to judge how other investors are explaining narratives.

WSJ: Is that kind of like economist's John Maynard Keynes's idea that investing in stocks is like trying to judge how others are judging a beauty contest?

MR. SHILLER: Yes. In the dot-com controversy, Amazon was a genuine success. But others just looked superficially like Amazon and were riding along the narrative.

WSJ: Are there any narratives you see developing soon?

MR. SHILLER: I mention nine perennial ones in my book: panic versus confidence; frugality versus conspicuous consumption; gold standard versus bimetallism; labor-saving machines replace many jobs; automation and artificial intelligence replace almost all jobs; real-estate booms and busts; stock-market bubbles; boycotts, profiteers and evil business; the wage-price spiral and evil labor unions [or the idea that labor unions boost inflation].

---

Mr. Weil is a writer in West Palm Beach, Fla. He can be reached at reports@wsj.com.

Journal Reports: Funds & ETFs
Markets
'Funds of Funds' Move Into ETF World; These funds appear to be similar to regular ETFs. But there are important differences.

By Simon Constable 
1,219 words
9 December 2019
04:32
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Exchange-traded-fund providers are coming out with more offerings of what are known, a bit awkwardly, as "funds of funds."

While a regular ETF typically owns a basket of securities, an ETF fund of funds owns a basket of other ETFs. Funds of funds have a long history in the mutual-fund industry, and are now taking root in the ETF world.

At the end of November, there were 99 ETF funds of funds registered in the U.S., up from 48 at the end of 2014, according to Morningstar Direct.

These products can be split into two broad categories those focused on asset allocation and those dedicated to sectors or themes, says Todd Rosenbluth, head of ETF and mutual-fund research at financial-analysis company CFRA in New York.

The asset-allocation funds hold a mix of ETFs focused on stocks, bonds and other assets and "are essentially model portfolios in an ETF wrapper," Mr. Rosenbluth says. Examples include iShares Core Moderate Allocation ETF (AOM) and iShares Core Aggressive Allocation ETF (AOA). The former is weighted 54% bonds to 39% stocks. The latter is weighted 19% bonds, 79% stocks.

Other funds of funds specialize in themes or sectors. Global X Thematic Growth ETF (GXTG), which launched in November, is designed to tap into the disruptive trends seen in the economy, such as artificial intelligence and e-commerce. The fund owns seven funds drawn from Global-X's roster of 17 thematic ETFs. The holdings are systematically rebalanced and switched depending on which subsectors are seeing the highest sales growth.

"I think it's a great concept," says Mr. Rosenbluth. "The thematic ETFs require confidence in the long-term horizon of these themes, but the short-term performance can be bumpy, and the Global X fund gives you some enhanced diversification."

On the face of it, ETF funds of funds look, feel and perform like regular ETFs. But there are differences that are important to understand.

Why buy?

The primary appeal of an ETF fund of funds is to provide a one-stop investing solution that financial advisers can suggest to smaller investors, says Ben Johnson, director of global ETF research at Morningstar in Chicago. Such funds can make life easier for individuals who don't want to spend a lot of time thinking about their investments.

Many individual investors lack discipline when it comes to dealing with their own money. "How many of us have the time to do that in our lives?" says Noel Archard, global head of SPDR products at State Street Global Advisors in Boston. When people get busy, he says, investment management often goes on the back burner.

That said, one-stop shopping may not be as appealing to those with a lot of cash to invest. "Those clients would be paying the adviser a lot of money to purchase a single fund," says Mr. Johnson.

What is inside?

Just because a fund holds other funds doesn't mean it's a good bet. Mr. Johnson says there's always a risk that you get a "salad" fund, which is something like tossing the contents of your fridge into the blender and expecting a delicious meal.

The trick here is for investors to understand thoroughly the methodology behind the ETF fund of funds they are considering.

"I think investors should understand whether there's a thoughtful, coherent overarching objective that is threading these funds together," Mr. Johnson says. "A target-date series or an allocation portfolio would be examples of funds-of-funds that fit this mold; a potpourri of disparate thematic funds would not."

On a positive note, there is a lower likelihood of so-called style drift with an ETF fund of funds than there might be with a similar mutual-fund product. "With style drift, you might buy a value fund and then wake up one day with a growth fund," Mr. Johnson says. That is far less common with ETFs because the vast majority passively track indexes. "They are much more stable," he says.

How much does it cost?

Most ETF funds of funds are constructed using ETFs from the same fund family, which helps keep costs down.

"By having this suite of funds, we can subsidize the fund of funds," says Pedro Palandrani, a research analyst at Global-X in New York, in reference to the firm's Global X Thematic Growth fund. In this case, the annual expenses are 0.5%, which is lower than the gross expenses of some of the individual thematic ETFs in Global-X's stable.

Without the in-house approach, a fund of funds provider would need to charge an "overlay fee," or an extra charge over what the component funds charge, which would make the investment substantially less attractive.

The downside of a fund of funds being constructed from ETFs of the same fund family is that it is rare for all of those funds to be the best in their category. Some may be top performers, while others may be duds.

Could an investor save money by investing in a bunch of individual ETFs on his or her own, instead of buying an ETF fund of funds?

It used to be that funds-of-funds structures helped investors save on transaction costs such as brokerage commissions. Earlier this year, however, many large brokerages decided to eliminate commissions for trading ETFs. As such, a do-it-yourself approach could theoretically save money.

That said, the commissions aren't the only cost incurred when trading ETFs. Bid-ask spreads add to the cost of buying and selling. Larger investors, which include funds-of-fund providers, are often able to get better prices than individual investors who buy and sell in smaller amounts or so-called odd lots.

What are the tax implications?

Regular ETFs are considered tax-efficient for buy-and-hold investors because they track indexes. Indexes don't change frequently, which keeps ETFs' capital-gains distributions low.

Funds of funds, however, have more potential for taxable capital gains, especially if they have to rebalance the component ETFs frequently and those holdings are volatile. For instance, a fund of funds with a mix of 70% small-cap tech stocks and 30% high-yield bonds, which is rebalanced monthly to maintain the target weighting, would likely result in more taxable capital gains.

"Just like any other commingled vehicle with a high turnover strategy, there are more opportunities for a taxable event in the fund, versus a similar strategy with lower turnover," Mr. Archard says.

So far, those fears haven't materialized. Of the 99 ETF funds of funds in the market, only five had realized capital gains, he says.

Mr. Constable is a writer in Edinburgh, Scotland. He can be reached at "mailto:reports@wsj.com".

Investors Are Subdued About a Quantum-Computing ETF; Defiance Quantum ETF has yet to attract a lot of assets. The fund's backers say that is because the technology is in the early stages.

By Gerrard Cowan 
408 words
9 December 2019
03:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A quantum-computing breakthrough announced by Google in October illuminated the technology's potential and an investment opportunity.

So says Paul Dellaquila, president of Defiance ETFs. The firm's Defiance Quantum ETF (QTUM) tracks the BlueStar Quantum Computing & Machine Learning Index, which is made up of companies involved in quantum computing, along with a number of closely related sectors such as big-data solutions used in machine learning. The $12.4 million fund was up more than 40% for the year to date through November.

In "https://www.wsj.com/articles/google-claims-breakthrough-in-quantum-computing-11571843751", researchers claimed that the company's quantum computer generated around one million random strings of numbers in about three minutes, far faster than traditional computers. Defiance ETFs says there are implications for this technology, notably its ability to enhance machine learning that is, enabling computers to effectively learn with data without being explicitly programmed.

The technology is still in the early stages, Mr. Dellaquila says. "We're a little far out before we really see companies' bottom line being impacted, but once we get there, I think it's going to accelerate very, very quickly," he says.

QTUM's relatively low assets under management the fund was launched in September 2018 could be due to a lack of investor knowledge of the area, and the fact that real-world applications are many years away, says Neena Mishra, director of ETF research at Zacks Investment Research. And while the fund has performed impressively, it is slightly behind Vanguard Information Technology ETF (VGT) and Technology Select Sector SPDR ETF (XLK), broader and cheaper technology funds that could be more suitable for many individual investors.

However, Mr. Dellaquila says Defiance "does not benchmark our ETFs to broad-based tech; we focus more on providing targeted exposure to disruptive subsectors."

Mr. Cowan is a writer in Northern Ireland. He can be reached at "mailto:reports@wsj.com".

Investing in Funds & ETFs (A Special Report): In Translation --- Alternative Data

By Simon Constable 
251 words
9 December 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
R8
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The term "alternative facts" has been roundly ridiculed since its introduction into political discourse in 2017. In finance, there is a similar phrase that investors should take seriously: "alternative data."

That refers to proxy metrics or information originating from unofficial or noncompany sources that individuals can use to gain insight into an investment.

When measuring something as nebulous as investor anxiety, for example, you need a proxy because if you survey individuals directly about it, "you may get a fuzzy answer," says David Ranson, research director at financial analytics company HCWE.

Mr. Ranson instead looks at credit spreads, such as the ICE BofAML US Corporate BBB Option-Adjusted Spread, which measures how much more that creditworthy companies must pay compared with the government to borrow.

Some useful metrics come from unofficial sources -- for example, consumer activity on the web. IHS Markit publishes alternative data, including the various purchasing-managers indexes, or PMIs. Investors are able to access to alternative data sooner than official figures.

Similarly, investors can get a reading on a company's health before it reports results. Eagle Alpha of Dublin collects data on consumer transactions, social-media activity and online searches that investors could use, say, to learn how much a streaming service's subscription base has grown or fallen. "Using that gets you inside the boardroom of the company," says Eagle Alpha CEO Emmett Kilduff.

U.S. Venture Firm EIP Hires Former Carlyle Executive to Lead European Expansion; Energy Impact Partners' investors include large utility companies, such as U.K.-based National Grid

By Will Louch

English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Energy Impact Partners is the latest U.S. venture firm setting up a presence outside its home market.

The firm has hired former Carlyle Group LP executive Nazo Moosa as a managing partner to set up an office in London where she is responsible for helping source investments in fast-growing European startups that will benefit from shifts in energy consumption patterns.

EIP also recently opened an office in Cologne, Germany, which is led by fellow new hire Matthias Dill, who also joined as a managing partner.

The firm will be looking to write equity checks of between  ¬5 million ($5.6 million) and  ¬25 million in the businesses it backs in Europe, Ms. Moosa said in written responses to questions.

EIP decided to open offices in Europe to capitalize on what the firm sees as an underserved market for fledgling businesses that need capital to scale internationally or launch new products.

"When we look at Europe, there is even a wider gap developing for companies seeking scale-up capital," Ms. Moosa wrote. "Anecdotally, we can see that not only are there fewer funds to start with but that as the existing growth capital funds become larger and larger, funds that can write cheques in the  ¬5 [million to]  ¬25 million range are becoming fewer and fewer."

EIP is the latest in a wave of U.S. venture firms to expand into new markets in recent months.

As competition for deals in the U.S. market has increased with established players like Sequoia Capital and new entrants including SoftBank Group Corp. raising record funds, more firms are looking farther afield.

New York-based Insight Partners recently opened its first overseas office in Tel Aviv. Lightspeed Venture Partners, another U.S. venture firm, also recently hired an executive to help build out a presence in Europe.

EIP closed its debut fund in 2018 with $681 million, garnering commitments from large utility companies that include the U.K.'s National Grid PLC and Japan's Tokyo Electric Power Co. Holdings Inc.

The firm has been investing the fund from offices in New York; San Francisco; Boston; and Palm Beach, Fla. It targets companies in technologies, services and products throughout the electricity supply chain from generation to consumption.

Although the majority of the firm's investment activities have focused on the U.S., EIP already has completed two deals in Europe in Norwegian analytics company eSmart Systems and Amsterdam-based fleet-management platform ViriCiti.

Ms. Moosa has over 20 years' experience investing in technology companies. She was part of the founding team of Carlyle's European technology investing team before leaving after 12 years in 2013 to set up C5 Capital, a European cybersecurity fund.

Economy
Watch Friday's Jobs Report for Clues on Economy's Trajectory; Bounce in employment is expected to reflect GM workers returning from a strike, while the jobless rate remains stable around historically low levels

By Sarah Chaney 
663 words
6 December 2019
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The Labor Department will release the November jobs report on Friday, offering a view of the U.S. economy's health as the year winds down. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal expect employers added jobs at a solid pace during the month while unemployment remained historically low. Here is what to watch for in the jobs report, due out Friday at 8:30 a.m. ET.

Strike Bounce

Watch for a bounceback in employment last month as General Motors Co. workers, who were on strike in October, returned to work. Employment in auto manufacturing fell by more than 40,000 in October because of the strike. Economists expect the return of GM strikers to help drive November job growth to 187,000, from 128,000 in October.

U.S. employment growth has cooled this year amid an economic slowdown. Employers added an average 167,000 payrolls each month in 2019, down from an average of 223,000 in 2018.

Under Four

Friday's report is expected to show the November unemployment rate remained at 3.6%, in line with October. The jobless rate has logged in at or below 4% for 20 straight months, the longest such stretch since the 1960s. The historically low rate is a sign that most workers who want a job can land one. The question now is how much further unemployment can fall. This year, it has stabilized between 3.5% and 4%.

Participation

Watch for whether the share of Americans working or seeking work continues to provide juice for job growth. The labor-force participation rate rose in October to the highest level since August 2013. Meanwhile, participation among those in their prime working years between the ages of 25 and 54 reached a 10-year high in October. The rise in labor-force participation is a sign that Americans remain confident in their job prospects. But a large pool of people ready to work may be one factor holding down wage gains.

Sluggish Paycheck

Wage growth is losing momentum compared with early this year, despite the unemployment rate trending near a 50-year low. Economists forecast the jobs report to show average hourly earnings advanced 3% from a year earlier, for the third straight month in November. Stagnating wage growth suggests the labor market isn't as tight as a 3.6% unemployment rate implies, said Indeed economist Nick Bunker. "Employers are still able to fill positions, often without pushing wages too much higher." Pay raises have been higher, however, for nonsupervisory workers. Their wages increased 3.5% from a year earlier in October. Wage growth for workers in certain low-wage industries, such as clothing stores and casinos, has picked up more than in other industries, Mr. Bunker said.

Temporary Slip

Temp-worker hiring tends to jump in the early stages of a recovery and crash as the economy stumbles toward a recession. Lately, it has been looking a little weak. Temporary-help employment peaked in December 2018 and has since fallen in six of the past 10 months. "Although we can only compare to two prior business cycles, this indicator bears close watching," Moody's Analytics economist Maria Cosma said. But this expansion, the longest on record, has shown it isn't always like other business cycles: Tried and true warning signals have set off false alarms during a manufacturing mini-recession from 2015-16 and again more recently amid an uncertain global outlook and another factory slump. Manufacturing's malaise could again be at play. An alternative explanation: With unemployment near a 50-year low, workers have the bargaining power to demand permanent positions.

Eric Morath and Jeffrey Sparshott contributed to this article.

Write to Sarah Chaney at "mailto:sarah.chaney@wsj.com"

Investors Chronicle:Impax Asset Management,Reach,Ted Baker

Investors Chronicle reporters 
1,496 words
6 December 2019
16:55
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Companies analysis from our sister publication

Buy:Impax Asset Management (IPX)

It is abundantly clear that Impax s sustainable investing mandates are both in demand and delivering for clients, writes Alex Newman.

The past year has been marked by  unprecedented concern globally about environmental issues and rapidly expanding opportunities , in the words of Impax Asset Management s chairman Keith Falconer. It is hard to contest that view. Equally, it is hard to think of a UK-listed financial services group better placed to capitalise on this momentous, even exponential, shift.

As previously disclosed, Impax saw its assets under management climb by 21 per cent to 15bn in the 12 months to September. While all but one of Impax s funds beat their MSCI All-Country World Index benchmark and contributed to the asset rise, the balance came from a number of new mandates. That outweighed a dip in flows to the Pax World business acquired in early 2018, despite big improvements in the performance of its funds.

Two years of growing fee income has also allowed Impax to pay off a $13m (10m) debt facility it signed to fund the Pax deal. Though a revolving credit facility has been retained, a strong cash position has led Impax to adopt a new policy to pay dividends equal to between 55 per cent and 80 per cent of adjusted profit after tax.

Broker Peel Hunt flagged the possibility of upgrades  as the year progresses , but currently expects adjusted earnings of 11.5p per share for the year to September 2020, rising to 12.2p in full-year 2021.

Hold: Reach (RCH)

Analysts at Numis expect Reach to deliver adjusted earnings per share of 39.6p in 2019, against 39.2p a year earlier. With the shares priced at 92p, this gives a forward price to earnings ratio of just 2.3 times, writes Harriet Clarfelt.

Newspaper publisher Reach is no longer in talks to buy certain assets from JPI Media   the entity formerly known as Johnston Press, whose titles include The Scotsman and The Yorkshire Post. Reach had been focusing primarily on JPI's regional assets. It said that while  active discussions    first announced in July   have ended, it will continue to review  on a regular and disciplined basis  those M&A opportunities that would accelerate its strategy. In the same breath, the group said that its trading has been  steady , and remains in line with its expectations. The shares climbed by more than a tenth in response.

For the five months to November 24, revenues fell by 4.4 per cent   better than the 6.6 per cent like-for-like contraction seen in the same period in 2018. Like-for-like print revenues were down 7.3 per cent, while digital rose by 14 per cent   constituting improvements from a fall of 8.2 per cent and a rise of 9.3 per cent respectively. To the latter point, Reach said that good audience growth had encouraged it to extend its network of digital regional brands into new places   with seven  Live  site launches planned for next year.

Analysts had previously given estimates for year-end net debt, but Reach   citing strong cash generation   now anticipates a net positive cash balance as at December 29. No bad thing, given the group s large pension deficit and progressive dividend policy. Overall, management is confident about meeting full-year performance expectations.

On JPI, Reach s chief financial officer Simon Fuller noted that  principally, when we look at acquisition opportunities, we look at the value for shareholders; and we had a price that we considered to be the right price for that set of assets, and we were not uncomfortable walking away if that s wasn t the price that was on the table. 

The same morning that this news was released, the Daily Mail and General Trust revealed that it has acquired the  i    a UK national newspaper and website   from JPI Media for 49.6m. DMGT   whose shares were largely unmoved following its own announcement   noted that in 2018, the  i  generated 11m in cash operating income and operating profits from 34m of revenue.

DMGT said that it was anticipated that the deal would be reviewed by the Competition and Markets Authority. Lord Rothermere   DMGT s chairman   noted that his group was  committed to preserving  the  i s   distinctive, high quality and politically independent editorial style .

Sell:Ted Baker (TED)

The 8 per cent share price plunge that followed the announcement of a considerable overstatement of inventory is aheadache the group does not need, writes Tom Dines.

Ted Baker has started an independent review of its accounts after discovering the value of its inventories had been overstated by around 20-25m over a number of prior years.

While the overstatement will not have a cash impact, it damages the clothing retailer s image, which it has been struggling to rebuild since its founder Ray Kelvin, stepped down in the midst of misconduct allegations in March. Shares dropped in October when the group unveiled an unexpected loss for the six months to August 2019.

The board has appointed law firm Freshfields Bruckhaus Deringer, with accountants expected to follow, to carry out a comprehensive review of the issue. The group did not disclose how many years the overstatement applied to and has said it will not comment further while the review is ongoing.

We highlighted growing stock levels as a cause for concern following the group s recent half-year results. It sat at 34 per cent of sales at the time, albeit inflated by the acquisition of its footwear licensee, and had been 37 per cent at the full year.

Broker Peel Hunt said the announcement would be  a bump in the road  for the average business, but  is the latest in a line of setbacks that continue to damage investor confidence  for Ted Baker.

John Hughman:Befriend the trend

Labour s plans to nationalise a chunk of BT have been met with pretty much universal derision.

Nevertheless, as the election draws closer and Labour s polling remains fairly solid, investors are considering what life might look like under a Corbyn government that would also renationalise many other natural monopolies such as rail, post, power and water, some of which form the bedrock of many income investors  portfolios.

National Grid and SSE have already moved some of their operations offshore to thwart any potential grab.

The thinking behind these moves also inspired a sensible question at our seminar held this week on the subject of diversification   how should readers position their portfolios to similarly protect their wealth from the possibility of a statist Corbyn government?

Yet the question also highlights one of the inherent difficulties in building a well-diversified portfolio   that tactical diversification against specific events with binary outcomes is difficult to do, and could be rather dangerous if the outcome we position for does not come to pass. Good diversification is about the long term more than the short, and should always be in place to protect against the many, sometimes unexpected risks that could come along and rock the investment world   across asset classes, geographies and, increasingly, the big investment themes that are disrupting business today.

I am particularly struck by the idea that when determining our portfolio exposures, we should perhaps consider the somewhat radical idea of ripping up the theory of old, and consider an entirely new way of diversifying   in short, aligning our investments with the way the world works and is changing rather than with somewhat esoteric ratios of shares to bonds that we determine according to arbitrary risk profiles and an array of risks we can t accurately quantify.

Themes such as resource scarcity, decarbonisation, longevity, artificial intelligence and robotics, and the growth pivot to Asia offer disruptive threat and opportunity in equal measure and should surely sit at the heart of all investors  long-term thinking.

The problem is that unlike established portfolio theory there are as yet no clear rules as to how to implement thematic diversification. It s a problem we will be exploring in the months ahead, and maybe the trick is to combine the traditional approach to diversification with a thematic overlay   sticking with tried-and-tested asset allocation models but making sure the assets we buy reflect the changing world.

Whatever we conclude, there is no shortage of ways to buy into these world-shaping trends, whether through forward-thinking trusts such as Scottish Mortgage, exciting companies such as Alphabet or Alibaba, or via the proliferation of thematic ETFs. Investors   and capitalism in general   seem to have a lot of enemies at the moment. Trends could offer some much-needed friendship.

John Hughman is editor of Investors Chronicle

Good day! The velocity of private-equity fundraising might best be described as turbo-charged over the past couple of years. The Minnesota State Board of Investment has approved nearly $1 billion in commitments to private equity and credit funds, as Chris Cumming reports for WSJ Pro Private Equity.

Also in the news, software-focused Thoma Bravo is pursuing yet another acquisition of a publicly traded company. This time, the firm has inked an agreement to acquire publicly traded education software company Instructure Inc. for $2 billion. The company is the latest in a string of public companies that the firm has acquired or sought to buy this year.

Minnesota's pension system is planning a slate of investments in new private-equity funds, including ones from buyout firms Leonard Green & Partners, Vista Equity Partners and Madison Dearborn Partners, WSJ Pro's Chris Cumming writes. Instructure Inc. Wednesday said it agreed to be acquired by private-equity firm Thoma Bravo LLC for $47.60 a share, or about $2 billion, in cash. WSJ Pro
Cyber Daily: Verifying Security at Key Suppliers Requires Diligent Checkups, Experts Warn

834 words
5 December 2019
14:20
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. Due-diligence surveys are a good starting point for assessing supply-chain security but can't be the only way companies vet suppliers, report WSJ Pro's James Rundle and Catherine Stupp. Health-care nonprofit Highmark Health delegates basic security checks to a standards body it helped create. Mastercard says surveys are useful if followed by personal interviews.

Also today: More coverage from our conference this week, including lessons from Capital One's breach, how Cox Automotive approaches cloud security, and AI threats such as deepfakes and model hacking.

Supply-Chain Security

Risks from business partners prompt deeper vendor scrutiny. While vendor surveys have long been a favored due-diligence tool for large companies to examine the security posture of supply chains, these provide a limited view, said Omar Khawaja, chief information security officer at Pittsburgh-based nonprofit Highmark Health. Furthermore, it is easy for a supplier to overstate their security provisions on such forms, he added.

"We found that there's almost no ability to really rely on the efficacy of the responses coming through," he said. The effectiveness of due-diligence surveys has been questioned in other industries, including financial services.

At Highmark, Mr. Khawaja knew he wanted to examine suppliers closely, but said assessing thousands of companies isn't feasible.

"If we want to review and assess and evaluate evidence for every single control, that in and of itself could be a $30 million program, because it would probably take us about a month to validate every control for each of those vendors. We can't do that," he said.

Read more about what works at Highmark and how Mastercard tackles the problem.

Big Number

38%

Portion of people in the U.S. who have been victims of identity theft or data breaches this year, according to a survey of 1,293 adults from One Technologies LLC, a data analytics firm.

From WSJ Pro Cybersecurity Executive Forum

What security chiefs learned from the Capital One hack. The bank "mostly did everything correctly" when it came to protecting its cloud assets, said Andy Ellis, chief security officer at Akamai Technologies Inc. But it wasn't enough to prevent the compromise of data belonging to about 106 million of the bank's credit-card customers and applicants. Capital One said a hacker accessed the personal information of customers stored on Amazon Web Services' cloud. The avenue of entry, according to investigators: a poorly configured firewall accessed by a former employee of AWS.

Transition state: What's interesting, suggested Mr. Ellis, is that Capital One was compromised not because it didn't try to protect itself, but because as a hybrid cloud user, it faced a complicated problem in integrating the cloud with its own data centers. It took an insider to exploit the vulnerability, he said. It is hard to get security wrong if a company is 100% cloud or 0% cloud, he said. "People don't design well enough for transitions," he said.

Lines of responsibility for cybersecurity measures are often blurred between companies and their cloud providers, leaving businesses vulnerable to attacks, security experts say. Many companies that used Capital One

as a model for cloud security are now saying, "If it could happen to them, what about us," said Patty Smith, CISO at Cox Automotive Inc., the owner of brands such as Kelley Blue Book. Cox Automotive runs most of its applications in AWS. Ms. Smith said the company worked closely with Amazon to identify security areas that are covered by AWS and those that Cox's own engineers needed to develop or buy from third-party security vendors.

"Application engineers are not trained to be security professionals," Ms. Smith said.

Since the Capital One breach, she said, Amazon has built new security features based on company feedback, including continuous patching of software flaws and automated fixes to misconfigured security settings.

Text-based deepfakes, model hacking among top AI threats. Deepfake videos manipulated using AI to look realistic are a concern for security experts. But a bigger threat comes from fabricated news articles, websites or other text content created by AI, said Camille Franois, chief innovation officer at social-media analytics firm Graphika Inc. "If you can just simply automate believable and engaging text, then it's really flooding the internet with garbage in a very automated and scalable way."

Celeste Fralick, chief data scientist and senior principal engineer at cybersecurity-software company McAfee LLC, is concerned about AI model hacking. Also known as adversarial machine learning attacks, this occurs when a bad

actor attempts to find and exploit vulnerabilities in an AI system. Research by McAfee shows that self-driving cars could easily be tricked into thinking a speed-limit sign has a higher number than was actually posted, she said. "We extended the middle portion of the three, so the car didn't recognize it as 35; it recognized it as 85."

WSJ Pro
Supply-Chain Risks Prompt Deeper Vendor Scrutiny; Security chiefs say simple questionnaires are no longer adequate

By James Rundle and Catherine Stupp 
874 words
5 December 2019
10:30
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Cybersecurity risks are prompting companies to come up with new ways of assessing the security of their suppliers, security chiefs say, but deep examinations can prove prohibitively expensive.

While vendor surveys have long been a favored due-diligence tool for large companies to examine the security posture of supply chains, these provide a limited view, said Omar Khawaja, chief information security officer at Pittsburgh-based nonprofit Highmark Health. Furthermore, it is easy for a supplier to overstate their security provisions on such forms, he added.

"We found that there's a there's almost no ability to really rely on the efficacy of the responses coming through," he said, speaking Tuesday at the WSJ Pro Cybersecurity Executive Forum in New York.

Levels of scrutiny should vary, said Alla Valente, an analyst at Forrester Research Inc. Create a catalog of all suppliers and the data they have access to, she said. Suppliers that process customer data or provide digital components in devices require a higher level of scrutiny, she added.

The effectiveness of due-diligence surveys has been "https://www.wsj.com/articles/cyber-risks-force-banks-to-rethink-vendor-relationships-11570468547", including financial services.

At Highmark, Mr. Khawaja knew he wanted to examine suppliers closely, but said assessing thousands of companies isn't feasible.

"If we want to review and assess and evaluate evidence for every single control, that in and of itself could be a $30 million program, because it would probably take us about a month to validate every control for each of those vendors. We can't do that," he said.

The solution was to move to a common framework from the Health Information Trust Alliance, known as Hitrust. The standards body, based in Frisco, Texas, was "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB122646645329820301" by a consortium of health-care organizations including Highmark.

Using Hitrust lets Highmark delegate basic security checkups, Mr. Khawaja said. The company's contracts require that a supplier be Hitrust-certified. This also ensures that suppliers keep up-to-date on threats.

"Three years ago, four years ago, we probably weren't thinking about controls to protect against ransomware, but we should now. So the Hitrust framework, every year, gets updated a couple of times. Control requirements go up and they change based on the risk associated with the specific context of the organization," he said.

The health-care sector is highly regulated, meaning that regulators are scrutinizing whether companies are making sure their suppliers protect data.

Medical providers make basic mistakes such as failing to have proper business associate agreements, violating the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act, said Roger Severino, director of the office of civil rights at the Department of Health and Human Services. HHS collected a record $28.7 million in fines in 2018. The department has issued more than $12 million in enforcement actions so far in 2019, he said.

"They must spell out every condition as to how that entity can use that data, what analytics will be done, what protections are going to be put in place to make sure the data is safe and complies with Hipaa," Mr. Severino said Tuesday during another session at the Pro Cybersecurity event.

Before signing new business agreements, corporate cybersecurity teams should ask potential suppliers which standard frameworks they comply with, said Alan Brill, senior managing director in the cyber risk practice at Kroll, a unit of consulting firm Duff & Phelps Corp.

Questionnaires give companies a written record of their suppliers' statements, which can be helpful in case of legal disputes, Mr. Brill said. But it can take a long time for corporate cybersecurity teams to analyze responses from all of their suppliers. Mr. Brill recommends that businesses ask for certification from a third party such as an accounting firm proving that the supplier complies with some widely used security standard, he said.

Other security executives cautioned against ditching questionnaires entirely. Alissa Abdullah, deputy chief security officer at Mastercard Inc., said these are still useful, as long as they aren't the only source of information.

"I don't necessarily agree that questionnaires and assessments aren't the way to go. We have a vast network of suppliers and so we rely on that as our first point of data collection," said Ms. Abdullah, a former deputy chief information officer at the White House under the Obama administration, speaking on the same panel as Mr. Khawaja.

Ms. Abdullah said her approach is to start with vendor surveys, then follow up with on-site visits. Every supplier for Mastercard, she said, has a relationship with someone in the company who can continuously discuss security arrangements.

WSJ Pro
AI Helps Humana Pitch Flu Shots to Customers; Natural language generation tool from Persado got more Humana members to open emails and click through

By John McCormick 
753 words
5 December 2019
10:30
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Humana Inc. has employed artificial intelligence to come up with persuasive language in emails sent to customers to encourage more of them to get flu shots and it is seeing higher open and click-through rates.

The Louisville, Ky.-based health insurer serves more than 16 million customers, including four million Medicare Advantage members. Medicare Advantage plans are administered by private insurers. These plans typically offer lower out-of-pocket costs than traditional government-run Medicare in exchange for members using in-network doctors and hospitals.

Seniors are at the greatest risk of coming down with the respiratory illness. Those 65 and older "https://www.wsj.com/articles/one-problem-with-flu-shot-not-enough-people-get-it-11573813801" and 69% of hospitalizations in the 2017-18 season.

The flu season usually peaks between December and February.

Flu shots are an important part of keeping Humana members healthy and lowering health-care costs, said Jennifer Bazante, the company's chief marketing officer. For years, it thought it was doing a good job getting members to take preventive measures, but wanted to see if it could improve its messaging and engagement rates, Ms. Bazante said.

In 2016, Humana started working with New York-based software company Persado Inc., which offers a cloud-based AI tool "https://www.wsj.com/articles/jpmorgan-chase-taps-ai-to-make-marketing-messages-more-powerful-11564482606". The following year, Humana decided to use the tool on its flu-shot email campaign.

For the email subject line, Humana employees came up with the phrase "Be sure to protect yourself and your loved ones this flu season." Running that language through Persado resulted in the recommendation that Humana go with "[first name] we're serious about protecting you this flu season."

The changes might seem subtle, but as Ms. Bazante explains, the phrase "we're serious" conveyed a sense that the company was committed to its members' well-being.

The results were significant. The Humana message had an open rate of about 20%, while the Persado message had an open rate of 31%. And more people clicked through. The Persado message had a click-through rate of 0.83%, more than double the rate for the original Humana message, 0.41%.

"We have found, through in other research as well, that the emotional element cause the consumer to respond. It creates a sense of trust," Ms. Bazante said.

The company said it can't yet measure whether more members received flu shots, but it is working on it. Still, the email click-throughs points to members being more engaged in their health, a precursor to behavioral change, it said.

When a Persado customer comes up with wording for a particular campaign, it enters the phrase into Persado's platform. The system analyzes the words to determine their meaning. Persado then uses a form of AI called natural language generation which taps a repository of more than 1 million tagged words and phrases in English, ranked by how well they performed in other client campaigns to create a series of new messages. Those new messages are then tested on real customers.

Persado analyzes the responses clicks, conversions or other engagement metrics and produces a message designed to generate the greatest response. The system can make suggestions in 25 languages.

"We see which elements work better," said Alex Vratskides, Persado's chief executive.

Forrester Research Inc. analyst Brandon Purcell said Persado's offering is unique. Until recently, he said, messaging was left to what marketers thought worked best. "Persado is changing the game by actually creating that content and deciphering which content is going to resonate with which types of customers algorithmically," he said.

Ms. Bazante said the Humana marketing team, which was used to trusting its instincts, needed to get accustomed to using an automated process.

"As you see the performance of those campaigns improved and the response of the members, and the engagement of the members, then it started to be much more easily adopted by other teams," she said.

Humana, which has worked with Persado on more than 40 campaigns, including one promoting prescription refills, is making plans for about 20 more.

Write to John McCormick at "mailto:john.mccormick@wsj.com"

News
Marijuana sector struggles to secure insurance

ROBERT ARMSTRONG 
833 words
5 December 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
14
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Tobacco. Premiums

Providers including Berkshire and Aon offer coverage but supply remains restricted

Insurance providers including Berkshire Hathaway and Aon have begun offering coverage to US companies in the legal marijuana industry, which was long off limits for regulatory or reputational reasons.

But investors and executives said many insurers remained cautious, resulting in higher prices. They argued that more widely available coverage was badly needed.

"One of the most miserable things I have to deal with for my job is getting insurance," said Tim Conder, chief operating officer of publicly traded TILT Holdings, a Massachusetts-based group that owns a variety of cannabis-related companies and reported $46m in revenues in the third quarter.

Marijuana is legal for recreational use in Canada and 10 US states, and is either decriminalised or approved for medical use in many others. Every part of the business cultivation, lab testing, distribution and retail sales requires coverage, from general liability to property and workers' compensation.

Reliable numbers on the size of the industry are hard to come by, but legal spending on cannabis in the US was just under $10bn last year while cannabis companies attracted $14bn in funding, according to BDS Analytics, an industry consultancy.

Neil Hitchcock, chief executive of Bermuda-based insurance broker Skyfront, said his estimate was that the legal US cannabis industry would pay about $1bn in annual premiums were it insured to levels normal for other businesses. The demand meant Skyfront was "incredibly busy" but "the problem is the limited capacity to take risk" on cannabis businesses in the insurance market.

He pointed to Lloyd's of London, where members of the market are not permitted to write cannabis insurance in countries where the plant is not legal at a federal level. This rules out working with US clients. The marketplace said it would begin providing cover to Canadian companies last year.

Five years ago, insurance was hardly available to the US industry.

Mr Conder said the situation was improving. Today TILT's operating companies use insurance from both large providers such as Aon and small specialists such as Cannasure.

Others pointed to higher prices as a result of the restricted insurance supply.

"If your business plan includes the word cannabis, expect to pay more," said Kyle Nichols, president at insurance broker Hugh Wood Canada. Even in a country where cannabis is legal, companies in the industry could pay five or even ten times standard rates.

Kyle Kazan, founder of California Cannabis Enterprises, one of the largest US cannabis companies, also has extensive holdings in real estate and said his cultivation facilities in California paid double the insurance premiums required for his pecan farms in Georgia.

It was a challenge to find insurers who understood the industry.

"You don't want your local State Farm agent doing this," he said.

He uses insurers including Berkshire and Kinsale for property and workman's compensation coverage.

He said that obtaining directors and officers coverage, which provides liability cover for senior staff and executives, and car insurance for his distribution operations was a challenge.

Erich Bublitz, who oversees cannabis underwriting at Admiral Insurance, a subsidiary of W.R. Berkley, said his company avoided writing D&O coverage for cannabis groups because of their complex and sometimes opaque ownership structures, as well as the tangle of state and federal regulations. "We don't think there is enough controls in place for us to feel comfortable [writing D&O policies]".

On car insurance he said: "Workers in the industry tend to be users of the product. I'm not saying everyone who is driving is high ... but auto is hard [to underwrite] anyway, and you add in that factor and it becomes too hard."

But Mr Bublitz said he did not think the rates charged to insurance companies were significantly higher than in other industries, and there was increasing competition for business.

"Are [rates] higher than people growing soyabeans? Probably. But there are additional exposures [in cannabis]." He noted that people rarely broke into buildings to steal soyabeans.

Berkshire Hathaway and Aon did not respond to requests for comment.

Another risk that insurers pointed to was the prevalence of cash-based transactions when people bought cannabis products, resulting in significant sums having to be carried by delivery people or held at retail outlets.

Cynthia Cleveland, president of California cannabis group Vertical Brands, said this risk would decline with passage of the SAFE banking bill. If passed, it would make it explicit that federally regulated banks are permitted to work with cannabis companies in states where marijuana is legal.

"Our business welcomes regulation," Ms Cleveland said.

'If your plan includes the word cannabis, expect to pay more' Kyle Nichols, Hugh Wood Canada

US cannabis companies struggle to secure insurance

Robert Armstrong in New York 
817 words
5 December 2019

English

Insurers remain cautious in taking on risk, resulting in higher premiums

Major insurance providers, including Berkshire Hathaway and Aon, have begun to offer coverage to American companies in the legal marijuana industry, which had long been off-limits due to regulatory or reputational reasons.

However, investors and executives say many insurers remain cautious in their approach, resulting in higher prices. They argue that more widely available coverage is badly needed.

 One of the most miserable things I have to deal with for my job is getting insurance,  said Tim Conder, chief operating officer of publicly traded TILT Holdings, a Massachusetts-based group that owns a variety of cannabis-related companies and reported $46m in revenues in the third quarter.

Cynthia Cleveland, president of the California cannabis company Vertical Brands, pointed out that this risk would decline with the passage of the SAFE banking act, a bill currently in Congress. If passed, the SAFE act would make it explicit that federally regulated banks are permitted to work with cannabis companies in states where marijuana is legal.

 Our business welcomes regulation,  said Ms Cleveland.

Hedge fund tycoon Steve Cohen looks to secure elusive sports deal

1,521 words

05:00

Welcome to Due Diligence, the FT s daily deals briefing

FT premium subscribers can click here to receive Due Diligence every day by email.

After a long affair with modern art, Steve Cohen is going downmarket.

The hedge fund tycoon (pictured below) is in talks to buy a majority stake in Major League Baseball s New York Mets, fulfilling a long-held dream to own a major sports team.

It comes just days after the Financial Times brought you the news of Silver Lake s investment in Manchester City owner City Football Group. Sport as an asset class is big business and it s about to get bigger.

Cohen, who runs Point72 Asset Management, already owns an 8 per cent stake in the Mets. The Wilpon family are in talks to sell as much as 80 per cent of the Queens-based baseball team at a $2.6bn valuation.

Mets fans should not be so quick to rejoice at an exit of the Wilpons as they will remain in charge for a time.

Under the sale proposal, Fred Wilpon would remain the  control person  and chief executive for five years, while his son, Jeff Wilpon, would remain in his role of chief operating officer for the same period.

There s a lot of interesting facets to this story, so let us explain.

The Wilpons own the Mets through a vehicle called Sterling Partners after they assumed control back in 2002 when they bought out the publishing company Doubleday.

But the Mets ran into some financial trouble because of investments by Wilpon and Saul Katz, his business partner and brother-in-law, with Bernard Madoff.

The Mets weren t Cohen s first pick in the major league. He first had his sights set on the Los Angeles Dodgers and was widely expected to secure the winning bid. He was trumped at the last minute by Guggenheim Baseball Group, led by Mark Walter of Guggenheim Partners.

In 2012 the Mets reportedly completed the sale of 12 minority stakes in the baseball team, each representing a 4 per cent interest for $20m which is when Cohen first came in. Fellow hedge fund billionaire David Einhorn had previously tried to reach a deal for a minority stake but failed to close.

At the time Cohen s former hedge fund   SAC Capital Advisors   was under investigation by the Southern District of New York. The firm eventually pleaded guilty to insider trading in 2013 and paid $1.8bn in fines.

Other hedge fund managers owning large stakes in sports teams include David Tepper, the founder of Appaloosa Management, who bought the Carolina Panthers American football team last year for about $2.2bn. Marc Lasry, co-founder of Avenue Capital, owns a stake in the Milwaukee Bucks basketball team, while the former hedge fund manager Jeff Vinik owns the Tampa Bay Lightning ice hockey team.

Now Cohen is close to joining the ranks with his very own sports team, but the beleaguered Mets could be seen as a consolation prize next to the Dodgers. Although Cohen, who is worth $13bn, has deep pockets to fund the team.

Ad industry: from mad men to sad men

Companies rely on advertisers to make products more attractive. But advertising agencies have failed to add the same lustre to themselves.

Chief executives at two of the world s largest media groups, WPP and Publicis, face the challenge of making their art relevant in the digital age. They ve got very different ways of doing it.

The old clichs   drinking neat whisky and coming up with one liners on the spot   are out of the window. Technology behemoths such as Google and Facebook have forced ad agencies to shift their focus to more, shall we say, boring aspects, like data and tech.

Arthur Sadoun, who took the lead at Publicis in 2017 (pictured top right), is investing in the firm s own data and technology to help brands  take back control  of their customer relationship. It s a service Publicis thinks clients will prize more highly.

Mark Read, on the other hand, who took on the top job at WPP just over a year ago (pictured top left), thinks owning first-party data  is not a nirvana . He s trying to steady a listing ship, paying down debt, selling data assets and trying to bring closer the traditional and digital wings of the WPP empire.

One investor who spoke to the FT s Alex Barker and Harriet Agnew for their look at the industry put it like this:

Sadoun is  a prisoner of the margin target  while Read, by contrast, had  slashed the margin and lowered expectations, which makes life easier at the start .

The two ad empires were constructed through relentless dealmaking by their once all-powerful predecessors, Sir Martin Sorrell at WPP and Maurice Lvy at Publicis.

With the advent of the internet, expansion efforts ran aground and advertisers have struggled to compete with the fast-growing business of digital advertising.

Still, Sadoun plans to press on with acquisitions   notably the $4.4bn takeover of the digital marketing agency Epsilon in April, which followed the acquisition of digital consultancy Sapient in 2014.

Meanwhile, his predecessor Sir Martin is fighting claims that he slapped the face of one of his old WPP protgs.

Was Cristiano Ronaldo worth it?

Cristiano Ronaldo is one of the best footballers of all time. But at 33, the Portuguese striker (pictured below) was well past his peak when Juventus paid  ¬100m (plus  ¬12m in fees) for him to join from Real Madrid in the summer 2018.

With the first season of his four-year contract over, FT Alphaville s Claire Jones took a look at whether the deal for the star footballer was good value for money?

As our colleague Murad Ahmed wrote last year, La Vecchia Signora   or The Old Lady of Turin as Juventus is known   paid not only for Ronaldo s skills with the ball, but for his brand.

Sponsorship and advertising revenues have indeed shot up, along with shirt sales and the club s followers on social media.

On the pitch, however, the results are mixed.

After helping knock them out of the Champions League in the quarter finals during his final year in Spain, Ronaldo was unable to take Juventus any further this time around. A big flop that saw the Italian club miss out on as much as  ¬35.5m in prize money alone.

Economy
U.S. Private Sector Added 67,000 Jobs in November; ADP report falls short of economists' expectations; large and midsize businesses post largest increase

By Allison Prang 
249 words
4 December 2019
13:48
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The U.S. nonfarm private sector added 67,000 jobs in November, missing economists' expectations by a wide margin.

Economists polled by The Wall Street Journal were expecting the ADP National Employment Report to show an addition of 150,000 jobs.

Large and midsize businesses reported the largest increase in new jobs, adding 27,000 and 29,000 jobs, respectively. Small businesses, with 49 or less employees, added 11,000 jobs.

"The job market is losing its shine," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics, in prepared remarks. "Job openings are declining and if job growth slows any further unemployment will increase."

The ADP Research Institute collaborates with Moody's Analytics to produce the monthly report.

The job gains were all in the service-providing sector, which added 85,000 jobs. The education and health sector added 39,000 jobs, the most added in a category on the services side. The worst industry performer in the services group was trade, transportation and utilities, which lost 15,000 jobs.

The goods-producing sector lost 18,000 jobs, reporting job losses across the board in natural resources and mining, construction and manufacturing.

The U.S. Labor Department will release its U.S. employment numbers and unemployment rate for November on Friday.

* Mountain State companies still face challenges akin to those of other startups not based in California or the Northeast, including a shortage of local investors able to lead large, expansion-stage financings and the need to look outside the region for certain skilled professionals.

Hello. The WSJ Pro Cybersecurity Executive Forum tackled complex issues on Tuesday, including the role of artificial intelligence in fighting cybercrime, the insecurity of the Internet of Things and what doesn't work in securing a corporate supply chain.

Don't miss our coverage of the National Security Agency's forthcoming guidance on cloud security, by WSJ Pro reporters James Rundle and Catherine Stupp. Another highlight: Equifax and Norsk Hydro executives describe the chaos in the aftermath of cyberattacks on the two companies.

In the coming days, we will bring you more coverage of the many discussions from Tuesday. Stay tuned. In the meantime, check out the @WSJCyber Twitter feed and our website for video, images and other material from the conference.

Cloud Security

NSA to issue updated cloud security guidance. The National Security Agency, amid a series of attacks that have targeted service providers in recent months, is fine-tuning advisories that will describe attack methods used by nation-state and advanced hackers and will lay out methods to counter them.

"We'll be coming out with an unclassified advisory on the techniques used to compromise clouds and some mitigation advice," said Anne Neuberger, director of the NSA's Cybersecurity Directorate, speaking at the WSJ Pro Cybersecurity Executive Forum in New York on Tuesday.

The guidance, due out this month, is based in part on the NSA's own experience with cloud computing, she said.

Despite benefits such as cost, using the cloud can also carry risks. "If you want to steal research and development from multiple entities, you can compromise one managed service provider and accomplish a lot more from an espionage perspective," Ms. Neuberger said.

Read the entire story.

Post-Attack Clean Up

Equifax, Norsk Hydro executives describe chaos after attacks. Cyberattacks often cause pandemonium and the cleanup can take far longer than the public thinks, say executives from companies that have suffered hacks.

Jo De Vliegher, chief information officer at Norsk Hydro AS, described the initial moments after the Norwegian aluminum and energy company became infected with ransomware in March as "pure chaos."

Speaking on the same panel at the WSJ Pro Cybersecurity Executive Forum on Tuesday, Jamil Farshchi, chief information security officer of Equifax Inc., said that the effects of a cybersecurity incident can stretch out for months. Mr. Farshchi defended Equifax's response to its breach. "You can armchair quarterback any breach and the response. I think Equifax actually did a great job in responding to this situation," he said.

Norsk Hydro and Equifax faced very different public reactions to their attacks.

Read the entire story.

Big Number

$124 Billion

Global spending on data security efforts in 2019, according to estimates from Gartner Inc. That is up from $114 billion last year.

More From the Conference

Businesses use AI to thwart hackers. Companies are racing to improve products and services with artificial intelligence, the Internet of Things and other emerging technologies, stoking fears that data is becoming more vulnerable to hackers. But rather than impeding the pace of innovation, these concerns are prompting many corporate security chiefs to accelerate the development of advanced capabilities, in a bid to turn the tables on attackers by better detecting the misuse of data and keeping it safe.

Like a hacker: "Artificial intelligence is a backbone of security initiatives," Camille Franois, chief innovation officer of social-media analytics firm Graphika Inc. Among other applications, AI is being used in cyberattack modeling, where smart tools identify security vulnerabilities in simulated breaches or hacks, Ms. Franois said. The strategy is to approach your own systems like a hacker, she added.

Omar Khawaja, chief information security officer at Highmark i Health, said supplier security questionnaires aren't accurate. "No better than a coin toss. Half the data is inaccurate."

Nasrin Rezai, global chief information and product security officer at General Electric Co., said many third-party suppliers "are below the poverty line when it comes to cybersecurity."

Christine Izuakor, senior manager of global security strategy and awareness at United Airlines, said that women are 50% of the cyber staff there, and minorities nearly as much. It took two to three years of concerted effort to reach those numbers.

Kevin McNamee, director of threat intelligence at Nokia, said that Internet of Things devices often are set up with no antivirus, no firewall. "They're just sitting there."
